Chapter one and twenty.
In the veins of the bowel that bleed and are cut, treatment for wounds.
The bowels are located in layers, either inside the abdominal cavity resembling pomegranate seeds or like grapes, and they have both small and large blood vessels that bleed continuously.
The bandage should be one or many and should be outside the wound, except that which is from outside the wound should be in lesser amount with less moisture. It drains water white or a little blood continuously and it remains on the color of the body.
Treat the one that is inside the wound by instructing the assistant to dig and clean it until the wound is exposed, and then you see the tendons. Quickly bind them with sutures or hold them with forceps, then cut it at its roots if it does not cling to the sutures due to moisture and wetness.
Hold it with a rough splint or a sharp piece of bone, or pull it with your fingers, then cut it. After cutting, place some pointed forceps on it to maintain its position or fix it as needed in the opening of the knee joint.
Then treat it with various treatments until it heals, if the wound does not open itself, instruct the assistant to inject it with a needle to irrigate and clean it quickly when it is exposed by the assistant, as the tendons outside the wound are easy to handle.
It should be taken with your fingers or tied with a linen thread and cut, then treated as previously described until it heals; for those who dislike cutting with iron, a solution made from alum should be applied, taking a thin cord and inserting it into the abscess.
Draw the pus to the surface with a lance and insert it into its origin from the other side, then twist the cord below the lancet and it will be resistant and tighten the pus severely and firmly, then tie the cord and remove the lancet; do this for all the abscesses but leave one untouched.
To release the pus from it, then place a cup coated with oil or wax on the abscess and instruct the patient to inhale; leave it until the abscess has fallen off. If the abscess falls, treat it with cautery and all other treatments until it heals.
As for the treatment of ulcers, it is often due to the accumulation of pus and the thickening of the tissue; if it persists and does not respond to medicine, then it should be excavated with a scalpel or fingers until it becomes soft and the upper layer that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it breaks at the desired point, if it does not break then repeat with a stronger splint until it fractures completely and softens, then treat it; for it will heal.
The second and eighty-ninth chapter.
On the treatment of fractured bones, non-fractured bones, and stiff joints and soft joints.
The ligament is a continuous band resembling the color of the body, found throughout the body and particularly in the soles of the feet and fingers, and it causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut what surrounds the ligament and bind it with a bandage or splint, then remove it and treat it; if you wish, you can stitch it as in its chapter, and as for stiff joints, one of them is a hard nodule rough on top of the skin.
It often occurs in the hands of children, and its treatment is to stretch it out and cut or burn it with a hot iron or medication until it falls off; if you wish, you can stitch it as directed in the text.
Ligaments can be cut with the instrument I have mentioned in treating joint stiffness, and stiff joints are also known as nodules that are hard and rough on top of the skin, penetrating deeply; when it is cold, it feels like a toad's venom on it.
In the body, all of it and more is found in the hands. Treatment consists of taking a catgut or strong sinew cord and placing it around the abscess until the circle of the cord surrounds its sides. Then turn your hand with the cord until it breaks what is surrounding the abscess.
Place it deeply in the flesh and drain the abscess from its origin, if desired, do this with a copper or iron wire on this image:.
Keep the top of the cord at the tip closed to make it easier for the fingers to grasp and hold it, and if you wish, place the abscess in one of the channels in the fist.
Third and Thirtieth Section.
In the images of tools used for lancing boils, pimples and wounds.
The lancing instrument may be made of brass, zinc or copper and may be hollow or solid, and this tool may be small or large depending on its use. The one used for treating small children or those with constricted or irritated anus will be very small, while the lancet for larger patients will be larger, image of a large lancet:.
The upper band that connects the sinew should be broad in this manner, having a stop where the sinew is joined, as you see, and the lower side that enters into the socket shall be narrow towards the bone on one side with two holes, and on the other side with one hole.
The cavity of the hole should be deep or slightly deep, and the sinew that receives the medicine made from animal horn or bone splints should be formed in the shape of a small spoon, its diameter being narrow.
Many holes should be drilled in a circular motion, and between each hole there will be a small notch, then thread should be passed through these holes with a thin needle from silk or similar materials and collect the sinew in the shape of a spoon. If medicine is placed firmly in the reamer between two stones, then it should be drilled with it. This is a circular representation of the sinew and the hole.
The method of drilling is to fill the bone or horn with medicine, keeping it as deep as possible and beware that it does not become too hot or too cold, then the plunger is placed on its surface and pushed upwards.
It is placed underneath (it), then the edge of the pincers is anointed with oil or egg white or something similar, then it is introduced into the cavity accompanied by a servant holding the pincers, and he grinds it strongly until everything in the pincers is emptied into the cavity.
Then the pincers are withdrawn and the patient is made to lean against a wall, for if the medicine is absorbed by the wound for exit, let him grasp whatever he can and if he falls asleep during the night, it will be as effective in the ulcer, for if the pincers are inserted into an ulcer and hasten its exit, then the pincers should be held until they discharge.
If there are boils or abscesses that are painful, then the pincers should be very gentle and soft compared to the pincers used for children, and this is a picture of a gentle closed pincer with its leather cover.
The Fourth and Eighty-Ninth Section.
In the Treatment of Surgery.
I have mentioned in the divisions of treating what harms the kidneys, and here I will explain their treatment by hand and with medications to perfection. I say that surgeries differ according to what is present in the surgery and according to the place where the incision occurs.
The things that are numerous in surgeries are as many as pebbles, stones or blades of swords or knives, or drills with bone saws or gouges, and so forth.
As for surgeries in various body locations, they are such as cuts on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and so forth from the organs.
I will describe the treatment of some surgeries to establish a measure and principle for all surgeries, and I will begin with simple surgeries of the head, particularly because compound ones will be mentioned in the third chapter of this book.
When a simple wound occurs on the head that is not a bone fracture and it was only skin cut, and the wound is large and one fears the development of hot worm, one should seek out the quinid (a type of herb or root) to its location. Do not delay that and extract from it (the quinid) to the extent of its strength, unless something prevents it, and most importantly, if the blood that is extracted from the wound flows easily, the extraction should be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warning: Abstain from overeating, drinking, and heavy food. If unable to do so, one should eat little and avoid meat and drinks. The wound should be carried with caution if it occurs and the affected area is a hot, swollen abscess in the fat of the thigh or with alcohol that has been seized.
If the wound is free of hotness, carry it with care if its blood has not changed into yellow pus for a little while, but if it has already changed, use some antiseptic.
Continue to irrigate the wound until it dries up, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and caused by a sword cut or similar and the eyes have not met its benefits, sew it up as I described in the abdominal surgeon's sutures.
If the skin is exposed and connected to the bone without any swelling, cut it off then treat the wound with medicines that induce hardening of the flesh and order the patient to eat foods rich in nutrients such as garlic and onions, etc.
If a boil occurs in the skin without it being connected to the bone, cut off all of the affected area and cauterize it, then treat it. In these types of wounds, if there are veins or arteries from which blood flows out and is not stopped by medicines, probe the veins.
If it (the wound) does not suppurate, make it suppurate by applying a plaster and bind it. If necessity requires and the remedies we have mentioned do not help, keep cauterizing until the blood stops. These surgical procedures are simple, so easy treatment is sufficient, letting them heal on their own and they will heal with their own blood.
Before it (the wound) is exposed to air, the recipe for the plaster is to take a part from lime, a part from alum and three parts of burnt or unburnt clay, grind them all into powder, mix it with water, strain and apply it to the wound, pressing it firmly until it adheres well to it.
The artery is tied off, then it is pressed from above with the help of leeches and left not to heal until there is no inflammation. It will not release medicine until it breaks out, and this can be done by clay if lime and alum are not available.
This action can also be done in many major surgeries if the position is mastered and it is pressed and sealed, and these surgeries were straight with their own blood as long as they are not exposed to air. However, if the air has changed some of them slightly or especially during the summer.
This passage in Arabic text by Albucasis suggests: "In this case of ulcers, it does not mean to treat as described earlier by applying certain pressed medicaments or a paste made from barley flour and water with honey until the pus flows, then treating it with other treatments until it heals.
In case of injury causing a bone fracture that is smooth, pull it gently, and I have mentioned the treatment for skull fractures in a previous chapter, in the surgery of the neck. If injury occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a skull fracture if it is simple.
If a nerve has been severed in the neck or arm, there is no remedy other than avoiding treatment that tightly binds such as tourniquets and similar items because they injure the nerve and cause pain.
A cold, hard object should not be placed on the wound as the nerve endings are cold and have a finer connection with the brain than other parts. The treatment for such wounds is usually mild medications like diluted vinegar soaked in hot water or olive oil, or tallow and honey.
If you wash with cold water and various metal stones on this kind of wound or a moist suture, and if the wound is large, use stitching or hold it together with bandages then treat until it heals.
If a hollow cavity occurs in the wound and pus accumulates beneath it, if a vein is cut and blood flows out, tie or sew it up or apply a compress if necessary.
If some or all of the hair follicles are cut and glands are opened, gather the skin together with stitches at the site of the cut and do not press on the hair follicles but sew and place it back in its natural shape then tighten it firmly and leave it for a few days.
Then treat it with sutures containing a binding and drying agent until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it. If there are splinters of bone present, remove them all or as much as possible and leave it to heal and allow it to be drained.
If the wound is linear, apply the incision described, if it opens in the wound a bone appears and becomes visible, treat it as described in its chapter, in thoracotomy and between the shoulders.
If the wound is either linear or slashed, and you observe a cavity in it, then if breath escapes from the injured party, know that it is a combat wound. If there is no cavity and it is linear with bleeding, do not immediately apply incision and sutures, nor press it for fear that blood fills its cavity.
Lead the wounded person to the heart and kill him, but make two incisions in it with a sharp instrument and close them during daylight for two times, if the incision does not appear, then make an opening in the mouth of the wound with a finger to drain any liquids that come out from it and let the wounded rest on the wound.
Remove what is accumulated in it, and if the injured has not improved or experienced swelling, redness, or tightness in the chest, and other conditions are normal, know that the wound is safe, treat it with ointment and other treatments until healed.
If the hand or the wrist is frequently strained, know that it has become paralyzed; treat it from its opening. The cause of injury is not to accumulate, and if it has reached the chest or back, treat it with what comes next in sewing.
If it is large or small, if there is a fracture in the bone and pieces of bone have been broken off, examine the injury and try to remove those pieces if they are free; if they are not free, leave them until.
The injury heals more easily when it is incised, and as for other injuries that occur in other parts of the body, their treatment should be decided according to what we have mentioned.
The Fifth and Eighteenth Chapter.
On abdominal surgery, evacuation of pus, and sewing it up.
The wound that opens up into the abdomen may be large or small and central; if it is large, pus or several pusses may come out of it, in which case insertion and sewing it up will be more difficult.
The small wound may also be difficult from another aspect; in this case, its closure must be more difficult and the pus, if it does not break out on its own at the right time, will swell and harden, making insertion more difficult. Therefore, the central wound is considered best because it does not complicate matters with the difficulty of closing the wound or the difficulty in introducing the pus into its place, as it does in these types.
Know that sewing the abdomen is done in four types of stitches, two general stitches are suitable for sewing the abdomen and other surgeries of the body, while the remaining two specialized stitches serve only for sewing the abdomen.
If the injury is small and something from the wound exits and it becomes blocked, this applies to one or both of two cases: either for the diminution of the wound as we mentioned earlier, or because the air was blown into the wound.
If it is as such, it is necessary to cauterize it by applying a wet compress or gauze in warm water and leave it there until the air escapes.
The drink that contains extract is better than water alone in the analysis of breath. If you see the breath has become foamed, pour boiling water with crushed or powdered glass into it; it will settle more easily when it enters.
If recovery does not occur after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound using the instrument that cuts veins and apply the first treatment again if possible, as the second application is necessary only when the first fails and when forceful measures are required due to lack of improvement. This is an image of the instrument:.
The pointed end of the instrument should be curved and limited, while the other end should not be limited, and its tip should not be sharp but slightly rounded. It resembles a small syringe as you can see. When the wound is wide open and water enters it, it should be returned to its natural shape.
Its special place is that if possible, it should be used in this way; as for when the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient must lie on his back and the person administering the treatment should raise his head and if it is located in the upper abdomen, the patient's head and chest should be raised from below.
And similarly, if the puncture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, make your intention and goal always to approach from the side containing the surgery, thus it should be used in major surgeries and middle surgeries as well.
And for minor surgeries, place it as you can, then prepare an assistant by your side who will hold the puncture entirely and close his eyes, then he should open it for the one in charge of stitching to take something after another.
This is the general stitching feature, to take a thread or multiple threads according to the size of the wound, then leave a margin on the side of the puncture equal to the thickness of the skin and sew one thread without putting thread in the seam of the skin. With the seam of the scalpel under the skin from inside until it is extracted from that side, and after gathering the skin folds and the seam of the scalpel which became four layers, then tighten with a thick thread around the suture several times until the wound edges meet firmly.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also and sew another thread, then tie it with the thread as you did with the first suture, keep doing this with as many threads as needed until the wound is completely filled and the suture is evenly spaced between the skin and the wound.
The pointed probe should be long and thin so as not to cut the flesh or muscle, and it is difficult to enter the skin; therefore, it should be central in both the probe and the lancet, and the probe should not pierce the skin near the edge.
It is not good to cut the flesh swiftly and open the wound before it is closed, nor to keep a distance while stitching lest the wound be prevented from healing; then the edges of the probe are cut so as not to injure the patient during sleep or cause him harm by applying pressure from all sides when he grips the probe.
Leave it until you learn that the wound has closed, and this type of stitching with the probe is more suitable for minor surgeries because it may suffice in sewing it with one or two probes or something similar.
As for the second method of stitching, it is to gather the stitches in four layers, meaning the skin and subcutaneous tissue in one pass with a probe containing a continuous thread adjusted in both the probe and the lancet; then if you pierce the probe with this layer of four, return the probe to the side from which it started.
The thread should be crossed from the top of the wound so that stitching is done according to the herringbone stitch, which the surgeon presses with his thumb and leaves a space between each stitch after the small finger has passed through, and this stitch is known by all people.
With this stitch, a surgical incision was made on the leg of a man who had been wounded by arrows and the wound was deeper than a finger and it had burst from his side about 15 fingers from the middle of the side, and the wound was in the center of the abdomen.
He was brought back after he kept his wounds open for four and twenty hours, so the wound became inflamed in about fifteen days and they treated him until he purged and lived afterwards for many years, managing all his affairs, and the doctors judged that he would not recover from a fistula (a abnormal passage or connection between two organs or cavities of the body).
And it is amazing that I did not treat him with medicine because I was in a place where there was nothing for medicines, so I used plain cotton on the wound twice a day and dedicated myself to wash it with water of honey until he recovered.
And as for one of the types of specific stitching, I remember it from the speech of Galenus and it is that the stitching starts with the skin and the thread enters from outside to inside, so if the thread goes into the skin and into the golden muscle that runs along the length of the abdomen, all of it.
Leave the probe in the side into which you inserted the iron, and draw out the iron from its opposite side within the wound, in the other probe from inside to outside in the other probe from without to within. If you draw it out, draw it out again in the same probe from without to within from outside.
Leave the probe in the side where it is, and draw out the iron from its opposite side within the wound, and draw it along with your pulling on it in the probe until you have drawn all of it out.
Then, start from this side and guide the probe along with the one in the other side where the iron is, and draw out the iron from the skin that is nearby, then return it to that skin, and guide the probe of the other side along with this one from within the wound.
Draw it out from the skin that is on its side, and do this again until you have sewn up all of the wound in this example, and this type of stitching is called "sewing of the fringes for the fringe".
Sew the probe once from one side with the skin, and leave it alone from the other side until it is empty, as for the second type of unique stitching that Al-Zahrawi also mentioned, this is his speech.
The translation of the excerpt is: "It should be sutured similarly to how surgeons of a certain group do, by gathering each part with its corresponding problematic area. Bring together the edges of the incision to the other and do the same for the edges of the wound. This should be done according to what has been described for you.
"It should be sutured from outside the wound edge to inside it and then back out, and then do the same for the other side of the incision. Repeat this process.
"Sew it from inside out on the other side of the incision, which is on the opposite side, and this is a better stitch than the common easy stitch that has been described. This is the opinion of Galenus, and some have said that abdominal suturing can be done with two sutures. "This means inserting a single suture and beginning by sewing the first suture from your side to the other side, then sewing the second suture from the opposite side back to you, following the herringbone stitch.
Know that if a wound is in the middle of the abdomen, its suturing will be more difficult than in other locations within the abdomen, and as for closing a wound with suture, it should be done according to the rules of all surgeries. This is because if you find the wound clean with its blood before the air changes it and you reap the fat (around the wound) and mark it and judge (the position), then lay the ligature upon it.
If the wound has been affected by air, lay some thongs upon it until it becomes firm and the sutures drop out and the pus and mucus settle, then treat it as you would any other surgery until it heals.
If no medication is available, lay cotton wool on it from the time it starts to weep and change it twice a day as you know until it heals. In most cases, additional treatment will not be necessary if the surgeries are simple; however, if you fear that the major organs may share in the pain of the wound.
It is necessary to apply a soft cloth in warm oil or lard and place it around the areas between the nipples and pubis, as heat will relieve pain or swelling in those areas. If you suspect there may be bruising or inflammation, give it frequent hot black draughts.
It is not in doubt that if ulceration has reached the cavity and becomes an opening into its depth, then know that what was from the cavities was hard, it is easier to heal, and what was from them was delicate, it is harder to heal.
As for the known ulceration, known as sciatica, it does not accept healing by surgery that takes place near the buttock, and this is due to a large number of vessels and its hardness, as well as the roughness of its wound and its proximity to nature of nerve, but if the one who has undergone the cut found relief, then treat the ulcer accordingly.
If it has passed a period of time and turned either green or black, then it should be compressed with thread above the place that is black to prevent bleeding, for indeed in the wound there are vessels and arteries, then cut what is not this [black area].
Make the ends of the thread connected from below the incision, outside it to make it easier for you to sew and extract it when the wound falls out and heals, remember the wound that is in the ulcer, if it shows a hole, small.
It is possible for some people to faint when they see a man who has been cut in the abdomen with a spear and the wound was on the right side, and the wound becomes suppurating and gas comes out of it, so I treated him as if I did not dare to heal it. I have not evacuated the pus until it has ripe and suppuration at the site, when I saw the place suppurating, I feared that something bad might happen to him in his depth, but nothing bad happened to him from this suppuration and he remained in the best condition. He is healthy, he eats and drinks and walks and goes to the bathhouse and is content in his service. Some of those with experience have said that when a wound is inflicted while the patient is small, it should be cauterized on this condition.
Take the large bees, then press their heads together, then place a bee from them, open-mouthed on the wound. If it is held and pressed, its head will stick and not dissolve. Then place another bee near the first one.
Continue to do this with several bees according to the size of the wound, then roll it over and bandage the wound. These heads will remain stuck in the wound until the wound heals and there is no fear of the bad effects of suppuration, and the wound may also be bound with thin thread.
The one who sows the animal skin which adheres to it after it enters the vein, this is done by taking one end of this thread from the animal skin and it should be stitched then tied in its end with a thin thread made of cotton, then insert that thread into the vein. In it is a cord of the Egyptians, he wraps with it the toe then directs it to the marrow. This strike is in the art of suturing and the Egyptians only perform it on the path of courage and hope.
If the wound is large and wide, and in no case if it is found in one of the toes, there is no trick or relief from it.
The Sixth and Eighteenth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Ulcers and Scabs.
Know that every wound or abscess that becomes ulcerated and progressive, and becomes a running sore without healing and the pus constantly emanates from it, is called in general terms as "Naswar" in any limb, and we call it "Zakam" (running sores).
This is in fact a hardened white bead without pain, having a hollow like the eye of a bird.
And for that reason, it is named a "bead" (Rishah), and it may be moist at times constantly emanating pus, and sometimes the moisture stops, but in some instances this moisture can be abundant or scarce, hard or soft.
It has been mentioned in the classification of surgeries that every ulcer that does not heal and there is no muscle growth in it, this is one of nine reasons. One of these reasons may be due to a lack of blood in the body or its insulation, or due to the presence inside it and on its edges of hardened meat that prevents the growth of good meat. It may be because it is much swollen and broken, or because the incision in itself is septic and the matter which pours out is not suitable for treatment, or because the drug is unsuitable for its cure, or because of a malady that has occurred in the town from the species of worms, or because of something particular in the town.
It may be because it is much swollen and broken, or because the incision in itself is septic and the matter which pours out is not suitable for treatment, or because the drug is unsuitable for its cure, or because of a malady that has occurred in the town from the species of worms, or because of something particular in the town. It is present in the city of Serqupsat, where diseases are difficult and tonsillitis is prevalent naturally, or because there is one or more bones, which have all been proven and their signs, causes and treatments with medications have been divided.

If any of the conditions does not reach these mentioned places (which have been described), let alone attempting to treat it.

When entering, it throbs and approaches the foreign body, if the wound has many openings, you cannot probe them all. So, push one of its ends into one of the openings, the moisture that you push out with it will flow towards the other openings and relieve them.
From it (the probe), withdraw the examination on any side where you can determine if there is a bone, cartilage or if the wound has narrowed due to swelling, and stop at every such condition with your limit of knowledge based on your understanding of its pain when you touch the location.
Among the reasons and causes of pain, if all these have been encountered and the reality is confirmed, then proceed to treatment with certainty. It means looking, if it exists.
If the navigator appears clearly near a safe position distant from nerves, arteries, veins, or any of the places previously mentioned, then break it open on what follows in the description and remove whatever decayed flesh, rotten meat, and excessive meat there is.
If it does not grow in the mouth from meat or pus and so on, and if treated, it heals, if the navigator is distant from the buttocks and remains stable, then break it open as deep as possible, then cleanse it of all rotten meat.
Then use a hot poultice in the medicines that are sharp and apply it to the navigator which you reach with iron, do this several times until the sharp medicine eats away all that remains of the corruption in the navigator's buttock.
Then force the healthy flesh to grow around it until it heals, if it does not heal with that, then cut it according to its description, if the cause of the navigator is bone and it is good for you, then probe it and break it open as you have been instructed, unless anything prevents you from doing so.
If you find a bone, muscle or limb (as previously mentioned), and you discover decay and corruption in it, remove the corrupted part until it disappears, then treat the remaining part with what cures it until it recovers. If it does not recover and the putrefaction persists as before, do as before.
Know that you have not reached all the decay, so discover everything on top of it and scrape off the crust and cleanse it with as much effort as possible, then force it to heal. If it recovers or else, know that this decay is in a hidden place distant from the decay, so discover everything on top of it.
Scrape off the crust and cleanse it with as much effort as possible, for if the bone was rotten, it was a small bone that could be pulled out using fine forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there were many bones, scrape and pull them all out without leaving anything behind with your effort. If something resists from the ligaments without it being in danger, cut those ligaments and the flesh if there was any and remove the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones in time, bear the wound and leave it for days until what surrounds those bones (the flesh and ligaments) decomposes. Maintain the wound carefully so that the wound does not become infected as a result of the hole you made during your work.

If one large bone, such as the shin or thigh bone, is fractured and only its surface has been damaged, grind it coarsely until the blackness and damage disappear, then force the fracture. Cut it to the point where the fracture ends, then set and heal it until it unites. I inform you of an example that happened to a man in his thigh so as to serve as a model for your treatment. This man had suffered from this condition recently.

I pulled it out with a hook, then applied medication and the swelling quickly subsided and disappeared. However, this procedure must be repeated for such cases of weakness or rarity, and fear of death due to its recurrence in all instances as a precautionary measure.

If you have finished incising from one side, then incise it from the other side, and let the incision be a little distance away from the decay as we said, if the decay is in a joint, separate the rotten joint itself and incise the bone where it connects from the other side.

Chapter seven and eight. (This seems more like a section title rather than an excerpt)
In cutting the edges and splitting bones: (This seems more like a section title rather than an excerpt)
In case the edges get inflamed either from external or internal causes, if treating the problem with medication does not work and the problem persists in the limb without any relief: (This excerpt is translated, but it's a bit long for an image caption)
It is necessary to cut that limb up to where the damage reaches, so as to save the patient from death or extreme pain, which is worse than losing the limb. A sign of this is that the limb turns black, making it appear as though fire has burned it.
After the limb turns black, the gangrene spreads further towards the body and it must be amputated. Similarly, if the cause of the damage is due to a bite from animals such as sharks, fire ants or rats and their ilk.
If the damage or bite is on a fingertip, amputate the fingertip and do not let the gangrene reach the hand. Similarly, if it has reached the hand, amputate it from its root and do not let the gangrene spread to the forearm.
Similarly, if it has taken hold in the forearm, amputate the forearm at the joint itself. If the gangrene is visible and appears to be approaching the elbow, do not amputate the whole arm as this would lead to the patient's death. Instead, use other treatments within your ability.
Similarly, for the leg if the gangrene is in the fingertip, amputate the fingertip at one of the joints. If it has taken hold in the calf, amputate the leg at its tendon. If it reaches the knee, amputate the leg below the knee.
If the wound has reached above the hip, there is no cure except to leave it and let the patient die. The recipe for amputation or incision is to tighten ligatures below the desired location and then tighten another ligature above it.
The servant should pull one ligature downward and another servant should pull the upper ligature upwards, making the meat between the ligatures bulge with a wide forceps until all the flesh is exposed. Then it should be cut or incised. It is recommended that pieces of charcoal be inserted from all sides.
It is not permissible for the saw to touch the correct location, as it may cause excessive pain and heat to the patient. If bleeding occurs during the procedure, quickly stop or apply some hemostatic agents before continuing with the treatment.
Then bind the wounded limb with ligatures that are suitable for it and treat it until it heals, and I inform you of an example: a man presenting his foot to you with his eye socket exposed as if it had been scooped out, which happened to a man whose foot was burned like fire.
That wound first occurred in his finger, and the man tried to pull it with all his might when he saw the wound, causing further pain and burn. It was then amputated at the joint and it healed.
When a long time had passed, the corruption became apparent in his little finger of the hand. He summoned me and applied the remedy he had on his hand with medicines. However, the remedy did not take effect and he began to suffer in the other finger.
Until the corruption appeared in his hand, he summoned me to amputate it, fearing that the remedy would fail and also fearing death during the amputation since the man's strength was at its peak, so when I hesitated, I departed to my homeland.
I heard that he had tetanus and had his hand amputated with caution, and I only tell this story to be of use for those suffering from this disease and as a guide and lesson on how to treat it.
The Eighth and Thirtieth Chapter.
Treatment of abscesses and the method of injecting them with medicines.
If a boil occurs in one of the flesh-covered parts and lasts for an extended period until it has swollen to such a size that it bursts or breaks, releasing all its contents and leaving the place empty, like an empty container.
The skin, similar to a blister, may have been scratched but has not yet deteriorated. It does not affect the bone, muscle or ligament. From this point it is appropriate for it to be called a cyst and not a nara unless its duration extends until it affects one of these body parts.
When it [the lesion] extends and becomes a nara or zekama, treatment consists of cutting off the entire skin layer completely as it rotates, and making sure that it does not adhere to the site for its deterioration.
If there is a possibility that the skin will adhere and has not yet reached its deterioration, the amount of skin that has a cyst-like lump of meat should be treated with lancing. This is by observing the cyst. If it is large and the unpleasant odor emanating from it is strong, lance it with the Egyptian medication.
The treatment is clarified oil, olive oil and honey mixed in equal parts, collected in a container and boiled until the medication thickens and comes in the form of honey. Then take as much as needed, wet it with water and honey, lance the cyst with it, and press firmly to extract the pus. Leave the medication inside for two hours.
Then, let him (the patient) evacuate during the day until the abscess cleanses and the pus disappears. If this medicine is not available to you, use water of ashes. Take either ashes from carob wood or ashes from poplar wood and pour water on it.
Clean it and irrigate with it until you are sure that the abscess has been washed, if there is no possibility of using Egyptian medicine or water of ashes, irrigate it with water mixed with honey and something from ground gall nuts.
Or irrigate it with honey and drink mixed together, because the honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while the drink relieves the abscess, especially if the drink contains an additive that solidifies and sets. If you do this several times and are sure that the abscess has disappeared, then irrigate it with something that meat grows in it.
Like taking from the gallnut (mastich), clarifying it with oil and water, and drinking a solidifying agent or irrigating it with some medicine that has been proven to have properties as mentioned in the article on medicinal plants, if the abscess is in the mouth and the cannula cannot pass through it.
He should be made to swallow a small amount of iron or place a soft lead in the Egyptian or thief's stone, until it expands. Similarly, if the mouth is also wide, gather your eyes with stitching and leave enough for the probe to enter without excess.
If the mouth of the one suffering from toothache is raised above, break it in a lower place to allow the toothache to drain downwards, because the toothache prevents the meat from growing if it is blocked in the sockets.
If you cannot break the socket downwards as desired, then try to extract the tooth easily based on what is convenient for you and ensure that nothing from the black tooth remains in it.
The medication to be placed on the socket from outside and the method of placing it are as follows: Take a problematic medicinal root, sharpen it into a sliver that fits the entire socket. Then, take a small spoon larger than the mouth of the socket and place a layer of soft medication on it, then place this layer over the problematic one and keep them apart from the mouth of the socket to facilitate the exit of the toothache and remove the large sliver only after.
The number of days [is specified], and as for the one located in the mouth cavity, it is the one that should be removed at every instance to expel what has accumulated in it, so that you may recognize whether pus from it contains a lot or little or is thick or thin.
Also, locate the mouth cavity itself, does its owner feel pain in it or not? Is there a pus or is there no pus, and is there an abscess or not an abscess. If your treatment follows this method and you see the substance that comes out of the mouth cavity to be smooth.
Know that if the mouth cavity has become attached or is about to become attached, then extract it the next day or the third day, and when the wound heals, replace the dressing and renew the linen. If pus comes out from the mouth cavity for a long time, do not be alarmed at its exudation.
Its pus is approaching, for there are some people who obstruct the meat of their wound and there are those who do not do so. If pus comes out from the mouth cavity for many days but it is not thick, know that the mouth cavity has not healed; the healing of the mouth cavity is prevented.
He prolonged the matter, he added in drying of the medications, so that it becomes a medication with moisture inside and in its strength becomes dry, like figs. If he adds excess, the addition is more of quince. And whoever needs what is treated by it should take figs.
It is permissible to use oil and lard, and then dip it in a strong drink, then grind it with it, and use it. Likewise, one can do this if figs are not available or if the honey-cooked figs are prepared until they change color and used or taken from the vine, mulberry, quince, and crushed all of them, and left to rest on the honey which is above fire. Then it is to be spread on a sheet of parchment or honey that has been cooked until it becomes clear, and alum is placed on top of it and pressed.
Various long zarwand, asafoetida, cinnamon bark, caraway seeds, and qanterion can be used alone or in a mixture to be crushed, sieved, and spread on the honey that has been described above, and it is used. The ninth and thirtieth section.
Treatment of abscesses, swellings and extra digits amputation and nail bed cuts.
Abscess is a lot of meat that grows under a swelling in the hand or leg and sometimes in other fingers, if it prolongs and treatment is delayed, it becomes inflamed and feverish.
A cavity or ulcer that eats away at the bone, sometimes it consumes all of it and reaches the bone, so that it has a foul smell and the nail bed becomes wide and its color turns yellow, if treated with what we mentioned in the previous section, but the treatment is not successful.

The incision (dowaali) refers to the deep cuts made in various parts of the body, particularly common in fingers and toes, especially in the fingertips, nails, and joints. In these areas, it is advisable to initially use strong black linen for stitching.
Then take the patient's basilique (a type of surgical instrument), and as for treating it with iron, one should strike it until blood is drawn and a black spot appears, while the other should smooth the cut and draw out the sutures. If it is not in a straight line as we mentioned, you should make cuts at each of the affected areas and the sites of the wounds, then tie it with snare until you pull all of it, and keep in mind that you must not cut the vein or injure it as this will make it difficult for you to pull it, thus saving your effort.

The First and Fortieth Chapter.
On the Incision of the City Vein (a vein in the brain).
This vein arises in the hot countries such as Hijaz, Arab lands and the few cold countries that have little snow, and it may also arise in other parts of the body apart from the sinews; it originates from an ulcer occurring beneath the skin.
As in the case of internal abscesses, buboes and boils that originate between the skin and flesh, a sign indicating the occurrence of this vein is that it occurs in the sinew and burns severely, then the place swells up; then the vein begins to issue from that swollen spot.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or animal; when its tip appears, it is necessary to apply a small piece of lead weighing a dirhem and a half to two dirhems and tie it up, leaving the lead attached to the sinew.
Whenever something comes out of it (the body) to the outside, tie it with thread in copper wire; if it is long, cut part of it and leave the rest, do not cut it from its root before everything has come out because if you cut it, it will become short, enter into the flesh, causing inflammation, swelling, pain at the site, and abscesses.
Therefore, it is advisable to gently draw and apply slight pressure until it comes out completely from the end and nothing remains in the body; it may come out from this abscess in some people up to a length of five or ten threads.
It has been reported to me that an abscess of twenty threads came out from a man; if it is cut while treating you, pus will enter into the wound and it will swell up for a long time with the body until everything inside it dissolves and try to drain the site with medications for several days.
Then, treat it with treatment for boils; this abscess may have many stalks and if it appears in the groin or on the man himself, it will produce numerous openings and come out from each stalk. It should be treated as we mentioned in the chapter and progressively.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the incision on the umbilical hernia that occurs beneath the abdomen, it is called the Al-Bakar ulcer.
This disease is called Al-Bakar ulcer in some countries because it often affects cattle, and it is a small, single hernia occurring between the skin and flesh that protrudes into the body vertically and horizontally.
It becomes apparent to the touch when probed from one limb to another until it pierces, where it has not pierced in the skin yet, and it protrudes as an abscess with some pus, as it is susceptible to hernia and worms in the abdomen.
It is expected from the disease that if it protrudes into the body and reaches the head, or reaches the eye, it will likely not open and discharge, causing the eye to be spoiled. In this case, if you want to treat it and remove it, it can only be done by probing it and its appearance to touch.
It is necessary to tighten what is above and below it with strong cords, then make an incision on it and remove it. If it has sunken into the flesh without being found, carry fire to the spot until it burns it, and enlarge its expected damage to the eye as we mentioned earlier.
If you see it (the boil) having appeared on the head close to the eye, press under it firmly and then cut it open and remove it. It is advisable for the patient to cleanse his body with easy-to-mix medicines for putrid ulcers and preserve foods conducive to healing.
The third section of chapter ten.
In the opening (incision) on the disease known as the Nafr.
This disease called Nafr in our land is a dislocation that appears in some parts of the body, then moves from one part to another and you may have seen it as described for you.
Call to a female slave in one of the gardens, I opened her arm and blew gently into its vein, then after an hour you will see that gas bubbling up like milk rises to its source as quickly as possible, similar to quicksilver.
If a dislocation occurs from one place to another, the pain remains in that location and settles in the joint, then after an hour it will spread throughout the body until it reaches the other arm, then I made her rotate her entire body as you saw.
I am amazed at the speed of its transfer from one member to another, and I had not seen this disease in this way on this characteristic except that I saw a group finding pain that transfers from one member to another, and I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I was unable to accomplish this except that the woman belonged to the nobility, her body being hollow and veins visible; hence, the transferred wind appeared in feeling, but it should not appear on this scale among the sick or those with moist, hidden veins.
If you want to treat it and the owner feels pain, if it appears to you as we described, then press firmly on it and pierce it with a lancet until the trapped wind comes out and stops in that place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, treat it by drawing out the flesh and expelling the winds like mustard plaster and similar medications.
The fourth and fortieth section.
On the extraction of the sutures (or stitches).
Stitches (sutures) may vary according to their types and the places in the body where they are located, and as for their differences according to their types, some are large or small, some are upright glass or closed glasses, and some have three corners while others have four.
From it are those parts which have a tongue, and as for the ones which do not have teeth, the one that is in the limbs where the joints reside will be on joints; whether the limbs are those of the head such as the skull, heart, liver, neck, both testicles, uterus, bladder, and similar ones. When a tumor occurs in one of these parts and symptoms of death appear to you, I will describe them to you later; it is necessary to avoid extracting that tumor from it, for death usually happens to its owner in most cases. When these symptoms do not appear to you and the tumor is movable in the cavity of the limb, extract it and treat the wound; from the signs of the skull: if a tumor occurs and bone breaks out and fractures the nasal bone which is on the skull, then one should be aware that it may cause severe pain, swelling, and fever.
When these symptoms do not appear to you and the tumor is movable in the cavity of the limb, extract it and treat the wound; from the signs of the skull: if a tumor occurs and bone breaks out and fractures the nasal bone which is on the skull, then one should be aware that it may cause severe pain, swelling, and fever. Redness in the eyes and tongue swelling, confusion of mind, seizures, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears and loss of speech and sound, followed by pus-like yellow discharge coming from the wound site; it is like a boil and fluid similar to raw meat will flow from it.
If these symptoms appear to you, refrain from treating the underlying cause and removing the foreign body if it does not come out, and as for the signs of a foreign body: if it is located in the heart and is close to the left shoulder, feel it as though it has become encapsulated in something hard, not in something hollow.
Perhaps the foreign body shows movement resembling a pulse and dark blood oozes from the wound, followed by coldness of the sides and cold sweat and bluish discoloration, then know that death is imminent, with no hope of recovery. A sign of a foreign body is if the head is injured and bluish white blood flows from the wound.
When the neck is affected, its color changes and it breathes heavily and demands cold air, then if a foreign body covers the chest, it will be close to small openings and breathing will be very difficult with severe pain.
The entire abdomen is swollen and moves, if a foreign body reaches the liver it follows with severe pain and blood similar to that of the liver comes from the wound, and if a foreign body reaches the spleen perhaps food other than it will come out from the wound.
The case is apparent when there is a hernia in the abdomen and blood appears within it, and something has emerged from the incision or wound or fistula; there is no doubt about treatment for such a condition, nor for removing the hernia. If the hernia occurs in the bladder and urine comes out.
If something emerges from a wound and the pain increases significantly, it indicates that the limb is dying. As for the rest of the body parts such as face, eyes, neck, chest, back, thighs, legs, feet, and everything similar to them; they will submit to the condition more easily.
If a hernia does not cause swelling or pain, and the hernia is not poisoned; I will inform you of some cases of this type of hernias so that you can rely on them for your treatment. This includes a hernia that occurred in the eye socket of a man near the nasal cavity.
I removed it from the other side beneath the eardrum and he was cured, and there was no harm to his eye. I also removed a hernia from a Jew who had it under his lower eyelid; the hernia had twisted but nothing else adhered to it except its smaller side which stuck to the bone.
The tumor was large, composed of the compound iron mass without ears. A Jew extracted it, and no ill event happened in his eye. The tumor was later extracted from a Christian, and the tumor was Arabic.
He had ears, and they were severed between the Jews. It had been swallowed in his throat, so he was treated until it was extracted, and a Christian received it and was safe. Later, a tumor was extracted from a man who had it in his abdomen; we expected him to die from it.
After remaining for about thirty days and nothing changed in his condition, they severed the tumor and it transformed into another substance and was extracted, and he was safe, and no ill event happened to him. I saw a man with a tumor on his chest, and the wound was inflicted upon him.
After seven years, the tumor came out in its original form as a hand. I saw a woman who had a tumor in her abdomen that was a tumor and the wound was inflicted upon her. The tumor remained and nothing changed in her condition or any danger was found for her in anything of her natural actions.
You see a man whose cheek is swollen and the wound bleeding, yet he feels little pain. Similar cases are many. I extracted a tooth from a man among the leaders of the ruler, who had it situated in the middle of his left nostril slightly.
I extracted the entire tooth after three days, then searched for the wound which was very tight. I probed it with a thin wire and did not feel anything, but he complained of pain and swelling below his right ear.
I hoped that the pain was from the tooth, so I pressed gently on the spot with my fingers. It produced a strong attraction and he winced slightly as if indicating the tooth had reached the spot. However, no event occurred that would indicate the tooth had passed through the spot. This suggests the tooth has not yet reached its position.
I pressed the wound with a pledget for several days, but no event occurred during that time. The pain subsided over the course of extracting the tooth and he remained calm until I felt the tooth inside his nose one day. I informed him and applied a sharp, edible medicine to the wound for several days.
Until it opens and its edge is felt, then the delicate hook that adheres to wood is sensed, then pressure was applied in the incision with that sharp drug until the edge of the splinter appeared to us by the eyes, and it stayed for a period of time similar to four months.
Then, when the wound expands and allows the forceps to enter it, they attract it and move it, but it did not respond to extraction. I did not forcefully remove it, but I tortured it with tools until a strong pair of forceps caught hold of it according to its appearance in this last section.
The physicians ruled that the neck of an ulcer should not be pressed, but I pressed it and compressed the wound and forced the abscess to burst violently without any harm to the patient, and I inform you about the method of extracting some splinters for this purpose to serve as a measure and indication of what I did not mention.
Because these parts of this craft and their details cannot be described or fully explained in a book, the skilled craftsman must gauge much with little, and what is apparent to him is what was missing, and he devises new works and new tools during unusual circumstances.
If you depart from this craft, I say that the wound only comes out of the parts where it has erupted in two ways, either by attraction to the place from which you entered or by confrontation with the opposite side, and it comes out through the place where you entered.
Either the wound is deep and located in a muscle, where it attracts and comes out; if it does not require immediate exit, you should wait for the surrounding meat to soften, making its removal and extraction easier, and this also applies if it occurs in bone.
You should also leave it for days and keep applying pressure and moving it every day, as it will come out; if it does not need to be extracted after days, you must drill around the wound in the same bone from all sides with a light chisel until it widens, then attract and extract it.
If the wound occurs in the bone of the skull and shows some of the symptoms that I have mentioned to you, grasp the wound and leave it until the matter resolves after several days.
Apply for the extraction of thorn or splinter, if it is deep and you are not able to draw it out, then use tweezers around the thorn as I have described to you, then treat the site until it heals; but if the thorn has worked its way into a body part. Feel for the pain and probe it with a probe, if you feel it then draw it out using some tools that are suitable for drawing it out; but if you cannot do so due to narrowness of the wound or deep location of the splinter and it is not bone, nerve, or vein.
Break it open until the wound expands and the splinter becomes accessible to be extracted; but if it has roots that hold onto them, remove the surrounding flesh from all sides with every method you can possibly use and then draw it out; but if you are unable to remove the flesh in breaking the roots, break the roots and separate them, then pull them apart until they come loose.
When trying to extract a splinter in any location, use tweezers on all sides until it comes loose and secure the tip of the probe so as not to break the splinter while trying to draw it out; but if you are unable to do so at your convenience.
Leave it for certain days until the affected meat heals, then resume treatment. If it resists, use the methods of treatment mentioned in the relevant section and preserve your effort from cutting veins or nerves or arteries and apply caution.
In any case, you can remove the thorn and this should be done with a clean hand and you should firmly grip it until you pull it out in its original shape as it was when the thorn pierced it, for this is more appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use whatever is possible for you in terms of methods. As for the thorn that comes out from the opposite side, it may either have come out with something outside or you find one edge of the thorn touching the skin from above and you see it protruding.
Break it as much as possible for the fracture to extend up to five knuckles, then pull it gently as this facilitates its removal. If it is lodged in bone, strike your hand on it repeatedly until the thorn moves in the bone and widens itself, then pull it.
If this is not possible, leave it for days until it comes out on its own. If the thorn returns, push it with a probe. If the probe has fallen and you intend to use it again, insert the straight instrument into the opening made by the probe, then push it with it.
If the foreign body is erect, push it back with a device that enters into this incision; for the foreign body will ease its exit in this manner. If the foreign body is diseased, it is necessary to cauterize all the meat surrounding it if possible, then treat it as appropriate.
If the foreign body is present in the chest, abdomen, liver, or flank and is near to what can be grasped with a hook, and you are able to incise it, then incise it and preserve from cutting vessels or nerves and remove it.
Then mark the wound if stitching is required, then treat it until it heals, the picture of the magnets which attract foreign bodies:.
The sides of it should be similar to the wings of a bird, made to resemble a magnet if it attracts a foreign body or something left behind, and they can be made in various sizes, large, small, or medium depending on the size and weight of the foreign body and the size and depth of the wound.

Fifth and Forty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Tying of Arteries.
On the Tying of Arteries. The arteries that are accustomed to be tied in the body, there are thirty arteries among them in the head, sixteen arteries known as the pulsing ones located behind the well-known ears (the carotids) and the four arteries in the visible temples (the temporal arteries), and the two known arteries in the prominent eyes (the ophthalmic arteries).
And the tied artery in the middle of the neck, and the artery on the side of the nose, and the two arteries in the upper eye socket, and the two arteries in the lower eye socket; these are the known arteries referred to as jugulars and those under the tongue.
As for the arteries that are tied in the arm and hand, they are five, one of them is the brachial (artery) which is from the venomous side and is commonly known as the head artery, and the radial (artery) which is central and is composed of branches from the basilic and the brachial, and is commonly known as the body artery.
The basilic (artery) is on the venomous side and is also called the spinal, and it is commonly known as the abdomen artery and the brachial vein, which lies under the arm and is tied there, appears above the elbow clearly, and is also called the tendon.
This is the artery that lies between the femoral and popliteal, it has two branches; one of them is beneath the knee cap from the wild side [i.e., outside], the second one is the scrotal and its place is near the heel from the human side [i.e., inside], and the female artery and its place is near the ankle from the wild side.
In the other leg, there are three arteries similar to it; as for the two arteries behind the ears, they are beneficial if tied for treating fistulas and abscesses and swellings of the head, especially the pulsating ones. The method is described below.
It is to cut the small artery at the elbow and then tie it loosely in a place where the two arteries are behind the ears with a coarse thread. Then, tighten the elbow joint with a tourniquet until the two arteries appear behind the ears in their depressed positions on the head. Feel them with your fingers to sense their pulsation under your fingers. Wherever you feel their pulse, that is where the tie should be made for instruction on tying.
Then, take a thin cannula, which is known as the needle, and insert it under the artery in the skin until it reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the artery and skin above and cut the artery along with the skin with a swift cut such that the length of the cut is about two fingers pressed together.
And take as much blood as you desire, then let it coagulate with the cautery and leave it until it escapes, for at the beginning of the book, they have been cut and separated. As for the varicose veins in the eyelids, it is beneficial to remove them for difficult cataract, persistent bleedings and ulcers directed towards the eyes.
The method of removing them is as follows: make the patient's hand warm with a heating agent until the veins appear clearly on the back of the hand, and then it will be easy to feel their pulsations. Once they are felt, learn where to cut them by feeling the surrounding area. Then raise the skin from above the vein up to the point you reached with your finger.
Insert the lancet under the vein and raise it to above, just as you did with other veins. Send blood according to your need, then dissolve the coagulum around the vein and place your finger on the vein for a minute.
Then place a bandage over it and press down firmly for a while, until it stops bleeding. They have already been mentioned, cut and tied in the beginning of the book. As for removing veins on the forehead, it is beneficial after removing the temple veins to relieve facial swellings such as abscesses, boils, and bruises.
"Method of drawing out a foreign body that has penetrated deeply, so that it appears; then take the instrument known as 'the forceps', and this is its image:" (This is a literal translation, please note that a more idiomatic translation might be different).
"Place the pointed end of the forceps on the foreign body and strike it from above with a blow or something similar, then let blood flow as much as desired; then release the forceps grip and pull gently until it comes out.
"It can be opened with a wide handle, but it should not be too narrow, for the handle should not be sharp like other forceps; rather, it should be slightly rounded so that it can be twisted when the bone is close, for fear that the forceps might break if it were too thin.
"As for the eyelids, which are two in number and are useful against the eye from irritation, redness, swelling, and facial diseases; and as for the method of drawing them out, it is to press the foreign body on its edge with a forceps then twist them and you remain on their head, and the pulling should be done by twisting in a slight rotational movement using a small handle.
"For a wound without muscle, if the handle is sharp, it might break; so send for blood as needed and place a dressing on them and press them for one night then release them; as for pulling the eyelid, it helps with painful swelling.
A strong headache, one of the diseases of the face, is like a red swelling that appears in the nose, especially if it's enlarged. Its treatment is to press its root firmly, then grab the nose with your left hand and take a thin and long forceps.
Pierce it in the center of the nostril, using the same instrument for gripping the nose on a steady hold, as nerves are not felt there, thus blood will flow from its artery. It is necessary to grip it slightly with your hand and send some blood, then tie it up at night; it will quickly constrict.
As for the boils, it is beneficial to treat them for suffocation and to start the bleeding and the sores that appear on the skin such as blackheads, carbuncles, pustules, and abscesses. Its treatment is to press its base under them with a band and let the maker stand on its tip.
The instrument is placed on a chair, then the nerve is stretched as far as possible and widened slightly, then an appropriate amount of blood is let out, or according to what seems necessary for that. Then it is done the same with the other nerve, then the band is undone.
The arteries should be tied firmly in the middle so that they are not cut, and left for later because it helps in healing the wound. As for the arteries of the neck, it is beneficial to tie them after tying the carotids because it benefits the brain in the mouth, decay of the gums, red bleeding from the eyes, and fractures of the eyelids.
To tie the arteries in the face and around it, first have your assistant stand in front of you and press his back against yours. Then turn your eyes to the arteries, one on the right side of the eye and the other on the left, and follow them with your fingers.
The same also applies to tying the arteries that are red and pulsating, which you should cut off immediately if they resemble one another, because if you are uncertain about them, cut off the larger one first and then tie the other as well. This is done in the lower eyelids.
As for the arteries under the tongue, it is beneficial to tie them after tying the carotids, for the use of these ligatures in diseases of the throat and mouth. The way to do this is to have your assistant sit between your legs with his head facing the sun, raise his tongue, and look below it on one side for an artery and on the other side for another. Both will turn red.
Extract and preserve them (the three veins) so that they are not cut, for beneath them lie nerves. However, if nerves are exposed from the extracted veins, then it is likely a blood vessel from those nerves will be visible. As for the three veins found in the arm, it is customary to extract them in two places.
Arm artery can be exposed by incising Rihan or Zaitun vein to the wrist, or by making a cut in the Sekkina vein, which is known as the Nishl (these are its images). Image of the wide Rihan arm artery:.
When it is opened as you see, it suits the incision of clogged veins filled with hardened blood that appear reddish and contain thick blood, and this is an image of the Zaitun arm artery:.
This artery is narrower and sharper at both ends, it suits the extraction of thin veins that contain thin, pale blood, and this is an image of the Nishl arm artery:.
This Nishl, which is suitable for incision, comes in various forms and thicknesses depending on the size and tightness of the veins, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three main veins, it is beneficial to locate it as it draws blood from the areas under the arm and neck, up to the chest and abdomen. It is important for the one who locates it to be careful and keep an eye on it; because beneath it are nerves. If he makes a mistake or adds in the incision of the back, that nerve will be cut.
In case of bleeding, it is important that the basilic vein is not incised in the back but should be located with a probe. If it does not appear clearly on the surface, it is necessary to avoid it and look for another vein or ask for assistance from one of the veins' branches.
If you want to locate the basilic vein, it should be done by feeling the arm until the position of pulsation is known. Then, learn its location by palpation before making an incision.
Then tie the arm and make an incision on the vein with a probe as previously mentioned, ensuring that the blow is at a distance from the nerves. When you feel a swelling in the location you learned by palpation, that swelling is the nerve. Avoid it.
If you see blood during a cut, it gushes out like a young boy's urine, and if the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from an artery. At that point, press for a while with your finger, then remove your finger. If the blood continues to flow greatly, it indicates that the bone has been broken.
Leave it and warn the patient not to move it for a while until it becomes stable, unless blood continues to flow excessively or you are unable to press on it at the time, in which case administer medicines for it. If the wound gapes open, know that its edges will contract and the blood will cease to flow.
Take a piece of bone, split it and press the half onto the wound tightly with ligatures and bind it for as many days as necessary, unless blood continues to flow. In that case, treat it according to the position of the ligature used for tying off the vein, and cut the blood vessel if not too difficult in most cases.
For small wounds where ligature can be used in the bone, know it. As for the vein that bleeds heavily, it is beneficial to tie it off so as to attract blood from the head and abdomen because it consists of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we mentioned earlier.
It is necessary for the leech to be on the back of the one who is being bled, because beneath it there are nerves. If more is added to the incision and it strikes the nerve, a mishap occurs in which pus forms that makes breathing difficult and perhaps does not pass easily; this nerve often appears to touch.
If it [the leech] is hidden in some people and is thin and not easily detected, it is necessary to make an incision for the bleeding in a spot where the nerve is avoided, since if there is an artery between two nerves, an incision should be made lengthwise. As for the arterial nerve, it is beneficial to bleed it because it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and specifically in this artery, if you want to bleed it; a nick should be made with the zest incision or the broad incision of cinnamon because it relaxes all the veins since there are no nerves nor arteries beneath it.
Except that, when bleeding, you should avoid the head of the humerus bone and seek a smooth location; it does not harm him if he is not hit with the first blow, but only if he does not bleed in the first attempt. However, in some people, if he is not bled in the first blow, he may experience pain.
But that worm does not harm him at all, as for the method of excision and its consequences, and what should be done before treating it, first it is necessary to know that excision can either be used for preserving health only.
If it is to be used for diseases or if it is to be used for preserving health and maintaining it, as well as preventing diseases, excision should be in one of the three veins located in the arm, namely the cephalic, basilic, and medial cubital, and excision should be done at the beginning of spring when signs of congestion appear.
Excision should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after passing three hours of the day, and if excision is to be used for diseases, it does not have a specific time but whenever there is need or necessity, either during the night or day.
In every hour and at every time, it is not permissible to excise infants until they reach fourteen years of age, and it is not permissible to excise the elderly who have exceeded sixty years, for whoever needs excision for any reason whatsoever.
It is necessary to clean the wound before cutting, with a thin needle if there is much dirt in it, lest it attracts to the veins during incision of the wound which causes an abscess that harms the main limbs; neither the drunkard nor the leper nor the bleeding should be cut until [the state] has passed from them.
To avoid cutting also after coitus, urination, defecation, increased amount of blood, fatigue, exercise, intoxication, fasting, or anything that weakens the body or soul; then observe the clotting of blood before doing so if the blood is thick.
With food, drink, and medication if possible; then the bath can be taken unless prevented by something or he desires some exercise to make the blood flow and make cutting in the daytime as we said and to keep his heart clear of all harmful psychological factors.
Like anger, fear, anxiety, and all bodily factors such as fatigue, uneven pressure, bleeding, and similar things; a meeting of the necessary items that people usually resort to for relief and comfort such as perfumes, incense, oils, and similar things should be prepared for every person according to their ability.

He should inflate the veins and make them visible to the touch, then he should rub the stump with olive oil specifically, then place his thumb from his left hand on the same vein in the location where he intends to amputate slightly so that it does not get damaged during the strike. The Seventy-Ninth Chapter.
On the Ligation of the Artery.
The ligature is mainly used in most cases where it is not possible to place an incision, either because of their small size such as the eyelid and vulva or because the limb is visible from the meat such as fingers, nose and surrounding areas.
