Chapter One.
In the first chapter. It contains explanations about breaking bones and their treatment before we start with the descriptions of fractured and dislocated limbs, one by one. It is necessary in this topic to mention some general rules and divisions. You are first obliged to understand and remain steadfast on its truth, for those who strive to learn this noble art, it is not for you.
If there is a fracture or dislocation, or a break or disjointment in any limb, it is necessary to hurry first towards setting or reducing it, or both of them. If there is no obstacle to that, such as weakness of strength or the one who suffered from it was a child or an elderly person or the time is very hot or very cold.
Then he should limit his diet to cold food such as chicken and mutton, and prohibit alcohol and heavy meat and avoid overeating, and every food that fills the veins with blood until you are assured that the infection is gone and there is no fear of inflammation spreading to the site.
Here, the cause should be returned to its original treatment, for if a broken bone is found in an open wound, it must be fed with food that nourishes greatly and strengthens, such as wheat, barley, leeks, onions, garlic, and the hooves of cattle. And egg, lean meat, thick drink, and similar things are among this treatment. With this care, the healing of the fracture will be faster and better. Also, know that broken bones in vigorous men and old people cannot be reconnected to their original state forever.
Splint their bones and strengthen them, and that which was broken can be reconnected in the softest state similar to the bones of small children, but nature will grow on the broken bone from all sides like calluses, becoming hard and requiring pressure to be applied to one part from another.
He connects them until it reaches maximum strength and solidity, as it was originally, until nothing hinders its action.
For this reason, it is necessary to make the food of the patient rich and nourishing, as we mentioned, and know that fractures can vary in type depending on the different bones; a fracture of the bone of the leg is unlike a fracture of the skull or a fracture of the chest unlike a fracture of the back.
Similarly, all limbs differ from each other in their specific ways, and we will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its respective chapter, as the nature of bone fractures can also vary within the same category.
A fracture can occur in different ways, some without the formation of splinters and others along the length of the bone. The nature of a fracture can also be with or without soft tissue injury, and it may occur with wounding and laceration of the skin, and a fracture may be simple or compound.
For each type of fracture, there is a specific method in setting it, and the characteristics of a bone fracture will be explained in detail in its respective place. Some common signs of a fracture include swelling, deformity, pain on palpation, and crepitus (a grating sensation) upon application of pressure to the affected area.
If there is no apparent deformity, crepitus, or pain upon palpation of the bone and the cause is not obvious, it is not a fracture but may be a sprain or strain. Do not attempt to move the affected area or apply excessive pressure.
If the bone is fractured and displaced without splinters, it means that each part has separated from its respective owner.
It is necessary for you to proceed from yourself to the examination and its preparation before a hot abscess occurs for it, if a hot abscess occurs, leave it for some days until the heat of the abscess subsides, then clean it with any means possible from the probes and lances.
Know that breaking and setting a bone is easier than a fracture that has become swollen, then it becomes difficult when he mentions it, if the bone is fractured, it is necessary to delay the healing of the limb from both hands or legs.
If the limb is small, it can be set with your hands, or using ropes and hands, or using a rope and hand, the position of the bone should be placed in its natural shape until the broken pieces of the bone extend, then press firmly on those additional areas with any means possible from lances and probes.
Make sure not to cause harm, pain or abscess due to your actions, strive to grasp one of the bones with its partner in the best possible way, during that time it is necessary to examine and feel them with your hand, if you find something inconsistent correct it and set it as much as your ability allows.
Be cautious of excessive incision and strong probing as many ignorant people often cause it, and it often happens because of them. A burn or a long-lasting wound occurs in the limb, as you have probably witnessed due to their actions repeatedly.
Then, he insists on the need for thorough examination and firmness of that part (the affected limb) after treatment, and warns against moving it during his waking or sleeping hours, at times of change, seizure, bleeding or any movement with great force or extent. It is also essential to avoid causing further injury to the limb, as pain may accompany it.
That is, when a limb has been set in pain or discomfort, it should not be moved to a different position where it does not experience the same level of pain. Instead, its original setting should remain stable.
In order to avoid causing pain in the limb, do not jerk it. As for how to set a broken limb, I will describe it to you. Keep in mind that the dimensions and parts of broken limbs vary greatly; some are small like fingers, toes, ankles, etc.
For the incision in abscesses, it should be narrow and shallow, not deep like a finger, palm or chest. The incisions should be firm and tight as the wide sutures necessitate the large organ and exert pressure from all sides, leaving no gaps.
After the settlement of your place, start with the suitable plaster to apply. In a simple fracture, start with the suture at the site of the fracture itself, three or four stitches depending on the size of the organ, and press slightly with the thread. Then move to the upper side of the fracture location.
Press slightly less than your strength to the fracture site, then move away from the fracture location with the thread a little and relax the pressure a little until you take something suitable from the correct place, then take another splint and suture it also on the fracture site.
Suture, then move the thread to the lower side of the fracture and perform suturing as described in the first stitch above. Then place between the incisions of a simple abscess or an abscess that matches the curvature of the fracture.
If there are no swellings, do not make any incision in it. Then break it with another tool and probe it carefully with a blunt, straight probe if there is no air or heat in the member.
If there is air or heat in it, press on it to release the heat and let it be for days. Then, when the probes are ready, push them into it. The probes should be made of a half-cylindrical, smooth, well-polished ivory or bamboo rod.
The probes are to be made of bamboo or reeds or calabash, and they should be tapered in such a way that each probe has this shape and form.

Chapter Two.
On the Fracture of the Skull in the Head.
Types of Fracture of the Skull in the Head are numerous, and their shapes vary widely, and their causes are diverse, for it may be a fracture due to a sword blow, and it either breaks all the bone or ends at the squamous part which is below the bone. Similarly to how splinters occur in wood.
And therefore, this type of fracture is called a splinter-like fracture or a partial cut of the bone with only one side broken but not completely through, and this type of fracture is called an incomplete fracture generally, and it can be either large or small.
And there is a type of fracture which may be a crushing or concussion, and its cause is a blow by stone or falling on a stone or similar, and this fracture can either be an open one that has broken through the covering under the bone or be in the face of the bone.
These two types of fractures can also be either large or small, and there is a type of fracture which may be hidden in the bone under the scalp and it is a wound that is easily overlooked.
And therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture, and it may be due to a fall or a blow with stone and so on, which penetrates the bone layer from the outside and remains concealed, as if exposed to corrosion by copper when it is struck, a part of it will enter into the interior.
That which is most common in the soft bone of the head is the fracture of the parietal bones, and it may be with or without protrusion. We will discuss its treatment in its place, and we will identify all these types of fractures by examination.
Identify it by palpation and remove the putrid flesh from over it, and as for the hairy type, it is recognized by its exposure of the bone, scratching, and rubbing over it with the probe; in this case, the fracture appears black. As for treating the fracture, first consider the symptoms of the injury.
If you see symptoms that suggest fear, such as convulsions, swelling, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, blindness, redness and similar symptoms, do not approach the injured person and do not treat him; in most cases, death occurs with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not worry you and suggest safety, then proceed with treatment. This is because if the injured person comes to you soon after being injured and this happened during the cold days.
It is necessary to make an effort in removing the bone before the fourteenth day, in every case and even if it is in summer days. It is necessary to hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day before what lies under the bone decays, as those symptoms that we have described appear.
If the bone is fractured and has reached the skull lining on the skull, and accompanied by swelling and inflammation, it is necessary to cut the affected part as I have described for you, which is to shave the head of the wound.
Expose the bone on any side that enables you and according to the shape of the wound, and whatever does not hinder on the wound. In case the exposure of the bone results in bleeding or a hot pus, deal with it accordingly.
Fill the place with plugs made of clay, oil, fat, and vinegar until the pus settles and the bleeding stops. Then take for setting the bone and removing it, and this can be done on one of two methods of work.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a thin and sharp blade like this:
Then use a small part of it (the incision) afterwards, here is its image:.

Because it (the chisel) does not exceed the limit of the skull towards what is behind it for the purpose that the chisel has a long, straight edge similar to the bevel or small circle which prevents it from slipping and piercing through the bone.

And as for how to make an incision around the broken bone, it is to make an incision on the bone and then direct it with your fingers until you know that the bone has come out. Then move the incision to another place and make a gap between each incision approximately equal to the width of the finger or close to it.
Then cut between each incision and do so with the aim of breaking the bone as much as possible, whether it be with your hand or with another tool from one of the instruments you have prepared for that purpose.
Similar to the pliers and fine tweezers, it is necessary to avoid touching the incision or the cut with anything from a saw, for if the bone is crushed and the saw adheres to it and extracts it, it must be removed.
Clean any remaining parts in the bone with another instrument similar to a saw but it must be sharper and finer than the other saws, for if there remains small bones or shavings, take them away with what you have at hand from the instruments.
Then treat the wound after that with forceps and probes, as mentioned earlier. As for the other aspect of the procedure, it is an easy stage far from danger, as Galen said and praised it extensively. This is his statement. You should first start by uncovering the bone fragment from the location where the fracture is most pronounced, until you uncover that bone fragment and find beneath it this osteotome shape: (Here, there should be an image of the osteotome's shape)
The osteotome shape should be slender, not cut anything, and the sharp portion of it should be on its shining sides as you can see. The osteotome shape serves as a guide for the chisel, and the sharp portion in the bone should be struck from one side.
Use a small hammer to cut all the bone smoothly as it turns, and you are safe from the danger of splitting (the bone), even if the patient is an expert or a novice.
If there remains something attached to the bone from the splinter in some places of the bone, remove it with the same osteotome shape and cut through it smoothly as it turns, for it will detach without harm or fear.
If the bone fracture does not reach the splinter and only a piece of the bone was cut off with its face remaining, and there is roughness and unevenness on its surface, it is necessary to smooth out that roughness and level out those unevenness using files of different measurements so you can use them in every situation according to its suitability and make it smooth as appropriate.
For broken bones and their shape, it is necessary that in your treatment you first align the fragments, then use a larger instrument on them, and continue to do so with smaller ones until you reach using the finest and sharpest of all.
For minor fractures and delicate breaks, it is necessary to treat each individually according to what leads to its healing. This is something not hidden from one who has knowledge in this craft. The least painful method and the cessation of movement are written and clearly indicated for major fractures.
If the dressing remains uncovered during your bone setting, it is necessary to take a pair of forceps according to the severity and to apply oil or fat on them, then place them in the wound, followed by taking another pair of similar or triple forceps. Apply it with oil or fat and place it on the first pair of forceps, then make this as gently as you can so as not to burden the scalpel, followed by using narrow bands from above without squeezing more than necessary to hold only the wound.
Keep him with this treatment for one or two days until the acute pain subsides, then dissolve it and use afterwards the medicines that are effective for consolidation such as gum resin, powdered coriander seed, powdered cinnamon and ginger root, and similar ones. Make these medicines into a paste.
Apply to the wound as it is soft, and in general, use a medicine that illuminates but does not burn. Exert your effort when treating the wound, so it remains clean. Do not allow fat or pus to accumulate, nor leave maggots to gather in it.
If maggots gather on the skull, they will rot and soften it, which leads to a great danger in such cases. The skull may appear exposed when bones are revealed, especially if neglected during treatment.
A blackening on the surface indicates that the symptoms mentioned previously have occurred, meaning there is no hope of recovery. If blackening occurs due to a medicine applied, it is because the medicine has such power to cause this blackening.
It is necessary to take three parts of honey and three parts of camel fat, beat them well, mix them together, then apply the mixture to the skull and treat it with various treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Treating a Broken Nose.
Know that a nose can only break due to the upper part breaking completely or one of its parts, because the lower part is cartilage and does not break. Instead, it becomes deformed, twisted, or crooked.
If one of his nasal bones is broken, it is necessary to insert the little fingers into the nostril and repair the fracture from inside with your pointed finger and remove the swelling from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. This should be done by you so that nothing happens due to your action and pain occurs.
If the break is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, repair should be done with a pointed instrument, slightly curved. If the break is on both sides, the same procedure should be followed, applying pressure on the first day after the fracture.
If swelling does not occur after seven or ten days from the heat of the wound, then enter a catheter made from cane into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two catheters if it is on both sides.
Make the catheter slender enough to fit into the nostril and remember some of the early practitioners mentioned that the catheter should be smeared with oil every day. However, I do not find it necessary, rather it is preferable to smear the catheter with white egg yolk mixed with flour from a mill.
Then, leave the fistula (opening) to allow the bone to heal and the passageways to harden. Afterward, insert into the nasal cavity tubes of red vinegar (Euzaena) after burning them with a linen thread. This will make its confinement stronger, so that the patient does not stop breathing.
It is not necessary to make this (treatment) or the fistula, but if you want to expose the nose in the course of your work and apply heat, then cauterize the nostril with kiryot or a cotton ball wrapped in oil and wax or something similar from the remedies of physicians.
If heat does not occur, it is necessary to cauterize it from the outside with fine semolina and sand that we have ground with egg white. Then, apply a linen cloth over it and do not tie the nose with anything because if the bones of the nose break, they will break in small pieces.
If it perforates, it is necessary to cauterize it and remove it with the appropriate instrument. Then, sew up the hole and treat it with what heals and soothes from the remedies that agree with this. If a wound occurs inside the nose, it is necessary to treat it with a fistula and use lead tubes. Until it bleeds.
Chapter Four.
If the lower jaw is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and not accompanied by an injury to the face, if it is only broken externally and has not been broken in two, and it sinks into the interior, its recognition is easy.
It is necessary, if the fracture is in the right side, to insert a splinter from the left hand into the mouth of the patient and similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, to insert a splinter from the right hand and lift the fracture particle from within and bring it outward.
And hold the other hand outside the bone to guide it in setting, for if the jaw is fractured and has split into two, it is necessary to use a wedge from both sides until it can be set, but if it has happened in the teeth it will grind them.
Or it may separate, so take a thread of gold or silver or brass, then put it on the fractured jaw and place a wedge on top of it, then put a large and firm weight or a piece of rawhide equal in length to the jaw on the wedge. Then bind it from above, according to what is suitable for its binding and compress it until it does not burst. Command the ill person to rest, relax, and make his food mild. If you think that something has changed in its shape from one of the faces, then prepare for its healing on the third day.
Then bind it from above, according to what is suitable for its binding and compress it until it does not burst. Command the ill person to rest, relax, and make his food mild. If you think that something has changed in its shape from one of the faces, then prepare for its healing on the third day. Then heal what you are compressing with the powder of pomegranate peel mixed with white egg or fine semolina after removing the scabs from it, and place a soft poultice on top of the compression. If that compression remains attached to it and does not change for the bone, leave it alone.
Do not heal it until it breaks open and the fracture spreads widely, for this fracture often spreads in three weeks. If swelling appears or a hot abscess forms, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until that abscess goes away.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, if it has broken away from the bone with fragments or shards, be gentle in extracting those fragments as you see fit using whatever tool suits you, for if there is narrowness at the site of the wound, widen it with a probe to your need.
Then, if you remove those fragments and nothing remains from them, draw a ligature for the wound if it is wide, otherwise carry one of the sutures that are suitable for that. Draw the wound until it heals.
The fifth chapter.
In the case of fracture, bind it.
When a fracture occurs, it is usually at the end of a bone and it breaks in one of three ways: either it splinters and fragments without causing any sharp pieces, or it causes pieces to appear and this is easier to bind.
If sharp pieces occur due to fracture, it is more difficult to bind and if the fracture occurs with a wound, work on it only if the fracture is not accompanied by a wound. Then, summon assistants to hold one of them above the broken bone and the other to pull the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, shape the fracture with your fingers until it takes the desired shape and there is no protrusion or depression. If more pressure is required, place a small ball of cotton or gauze under the affected area.
The bone should be as long as necessary and the fracture should be pulled and compressed with your hand until the fracture is shaped as desired. If it is not possible to pull the fractured end outwards because it has gone too deep, the lever should be applied to the affected area.
The fracture is situated beneath a knob in the bone, and the assistant should press it downwards until the bone of the prominence rises up. Then you will repair the break and set it using your fingers.
If you suspect that a bone from the prominence has been fractured and it is moving, it is necessary to compress it and remove that fragment along with it. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, it should be broken into pieces according to the predetermined cuts. After the prominence, the device that holds the saw is placed, and it is a tool made of wood or metal as shown here.
It resembles a spoon without a handle, and its width is according to the size of the bone it is intended for, while its length depends on what you can work with. It has two sides, one wider and the other narrower as shown by the wider side or the narrower one.
If a wound has been made during the extraction of a bone fragment and it is wide open and you have transmitted the heat of the wound, collect the edges of the wound with stitching, and if the wound is narrow or you fear the spread of heat, close the wound with the incision and apply the beneficial effects to the extent that the wound is closed.
If a boil or abscess is hot, make an incision in the skin around it and apply either oil, salve, or alcohol, then cover it. As for hardening of bones when it's neither fracture nor breakage, it should be treated by applying plaster made from oven ash mixed with white of egg to the affected bone. Place a soft poultice on it then place a cup under the poultice if needed, then take a long and narrow strip of cloth, place linseed oil or olive oil on it and wrap the poultice and plaster with it, then tie it with a thin rope from a smooth board. It should have a width of three fingers and be as long, then stretch it out in the incision, then place the cup in the same position as the fracture, then tie the cup along with the plaster at the location of the fracture, then tighten the plaster on the fracture as it rotates. And the cord should be placed around his neck and under the healthy side of the bandage and under the patient's bandage, and it should be tightened on all sides as you see fit, so that the fracture is tightly bound, which is not hidden from you.
Make sure the bandage does not loosen from the broken bone. It is necessary to examine the wound daily. If the sutures loosen and the abscess appears, correct it and tighten the sutures. Then make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pad under his buttocks during sleep at night.
Lift his arm away from the affected side to raise the fracture or dislocate the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck if it does not loosen within ten days of the incident, unless a complication such as an infection occurs.
Then renew the dressings if you observe any sign of deterioration for that purpose. Bandage and leave it until it becomes hard and the fracture heals, and it strengthens more in twenty days. It may be less for some people.
The Sixth Chapter.
In a broad fracture of the shoulder, only bones are broken, not the joint itself, and it is not common to fracture directly in the middle of the joint. This can only be determined by touch. Depending on the shape of the fracture, its form can be determined.
Straighten it and bandage it in its natural shape from all sides of the wound that you can, then apply flour of wheat with white egg and mild vinegar on top of the wound, then place a flat abscess made from thin leaves over it, and cover it with a wide, flat leaf.
Incise to the extent of the shoulders or slightly beyond, if it is under the clavicle, make an excavation in one of the shoulder's positions. Soften that excavation with a pallid cautery until the clavicle settles onto the bone surface, then tighten from above with a long forceps.
Tighten firmly and withdraw the clavicle until it does not move from its place, and every day check the bandage. When the bandage becomes loose, tighten it again and adjust the clavicle if it moves from its place. The arm should hang naturally on the correct side, and the shoulders will relax in 20 days.
After 20 or 50 days, if these number of days have passed, then release the bandage and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones that fear is not feared to break or dislocate. If a piece protrudes from the bone and was under the skin, break it open and remove it. And perform what you described in the case of a fractured femur for relief of the hot worm if anything like that happens.
The Seventh Chapter.
In the clavicle, case of a fractured chest.
If the chest is fractured in the middle, it rarely occurs and if its sides are fractured, they break more often and dislocate. Among its symptoms if the middle of the chest is fractured, it inclines towards below and exposes to pain, difficulty in breathing and suffocation.
"If a wound is made in the skin and it reveals a fracture in the broken bone, and it is not hidden to touch, he must bring the limb to the surface and make it lie between his armpits, then press the fractured parts together and collect the shards of the bone with both hands, treating each side as necessary.
"Then shape the bone as required, then wrap and bind it with bandages and place a splint made of slivers of wood or bone on top of it in the hollow part after breaking it. "Then treat it gently in binding the broken bone to prevent it from slipping, and pass the bandages around it towards the surface several times and tighten it firmly, then check the bandages at all times, and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it again.
"If necessity requires its solution in the case of an illness, injury or fracture, then treat it and remove the bandages and fix what is exposed by the method of treatment, then return the bandages if you see fit. "Turn him on his side and forcefully press him until he expires.
"Chapter Eight.
In the fractures of bones: If they break, it should be known that the fractures occur only in those places that are rough and are adjacent to the back, and pressure exposes them to pain because they are fragile. This is not hidden to the touch during examination with the fingers.
Apply pressure to it with the fingers on the face where it is possible until its shape becomes as required, then let it dry and harden the broken bone with a splint if that is necessary. If the fracture of bones is near to the inside, it exposes the patient to severe pain.
We break it like a fracture that exposes the patient to piercing pain because the bone breaks the covering and it also exposes him to difficulty, distress, bleeding profusely and this makes the treatment difficult, and the ends may change into various forms.
Among them is one who says that it is necessary to make food for the patient that does not produce wind and air so that his abdomen expands and swells, and the fracture is pushed outwards. We dislike this because it may increase the risk of a severe hot fever if it does not occur or if it has occurred, it increases and strengthens it.
Some of them say that it should be placed on the spot, then pressed strongly, and it resembles measurement except that he fears that the pressing will attract pus to the spot due to its weakness, and some of them say that it is necessary to cover the spot with a cloth soaked in hot oil.
The cure for the fissure between the anus will be to fill it until the ring is level, if the rectum is stretched over a roller, then treat the cause with the treatment of the ulcer from food and medicine, for if the cause is strong, it cannot be endured. The bone penetrates the abdominal wall causing pain and we fear the cause, so it is necessary to incise on the spot and expose the broken intestine beneath it, then place a tool that preserves the blade and describes its shape, and cut the bone with it and remove it.
Then we gather the wound if it is large with stitching and treat it with a bandage until it heals, for if it appears and the inflammation is hot, then apply the remedy in the oil of leeks and place it on the spot and treat the cause from within as well.
The wound is to be explored on the side that sleeps upon it until it opens up.
The ninth section.
Suturing of the forehead and neck.
If a bone of the neck is broken and it is not exposed, or if it is more exposed to fracture and also if a skull fracture occurs, then when that condition is presented to someone and you want to know if it opens up or does not open up. If he observes that his hands are numb, cold and lifeless and he cannot move them or stretch them or grasp them, and if you squeeze or press it with a probe, but he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that it will not open up in most cases, for it is dying.
And if he moves them and feels it in them with a squeeze or press, then know that the bone has healed and that it will open up with treatment. If a skull fracture occurs similarly, and you want to know whether it will also open up or not.
Look at his legs, if you see that they are numb and the same thing happens as in the hands, then when he lies down gas escapes from his stomach without his will and if he is cut open on his abdomen, urine comes out without his will. If it (urinary stone) has been ejected onto the back and the person intends to urinate but is unable to do so, know that he is dying; there is no cure for this case, as if it were not exposed to such a thing, the matter would be easier. Treatment for what has occurred from this (situation) is to apply warmth to ease the burning pain.
Place warm oil or a mixture of egg yolks and clarified butter on the inflamed bladder three times a day until the burning pain subsides. Then, apply one of the tight, cleansing bandages and bind it with a rope, instruct him to remain still and rest.
He should not sleep on the side that causes him pain until he recovers, as any pressure or anything that has caused pain in the bone must be massaged and the bone removed. Then, gather the wounded eyes if they are large with stitching, and treat them with warm poultices until they heal. If a bone in the jaw is broken and it prevents biting, then insert a probe from the left hand into the socket.
Straighten the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and then apply bandages and splints if necessary, and bind it tightly.
If you suspect a fractured bone, crack it open and remove it, then treat the wound with appropriate therapy until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
In case of fractured bone: crack it open.
When the bones of the back are fractured, they usually crack and break into several parts. The fragments will protrude from their original position, and if not treated immediately, the patient may experience severe pain and immobility.
Feel the fracture with your hand to determine its shape, if the fracture is along the sides only, then align it as best you can until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is in length or has moved towards the inside, place a pillow beneath it to align it, then wrap it with bandage and fix it firmly using a splint made of wood or leather.
Do not be afraid of the fracture shifting or the splint falling off. Adjust the bandage around the affected limb to keep it stable, and order the patient to lie on their back or the unaffected side. If exposed, cover with a warm blanket.
Apply pressure on the swelling and hold it until the hot worm settles down, then place what settles it on top of it as it advances, then return to the pressure and tighten it as necessary. If there is exposure in the bone or something protrudes from its sides, it should not be removed.
Do not press, but soothe from outside as we said and leave the pressure until it settles down.
The tenth section.
On bone fracture.
The bone is between the joints and the end of the arm; if it breaks, press on both sides with one hand taking a splint made of hard wood in the middle position as shown here:.
Tie strips on both sides, then attach from a raised position and the patient sits down on a chair. Then, the fractured limb is placed on the splint until it becomes fixed in the middle of the splint's curve. Then, something heavy is attached above it.
Or the servant holds it downwards, then the doctor aligns the fracture with his hands until it is in its proper position. The other face is that the patient leans on his elbow and ties his hand with a strap.
Then order one of your servants to grip what is above the fracture in his hand, and the other to grasp below it, and each of them moves towards his side. If you wish that the movement be stronger, tighten under the fracture with straps and above it with straps, and let both servants move towards the opposite sides.
If the fracture is close to the edge of the bone, it is necessary for the strap to be [at the center] under the shinbone and below the fracture towards the joint, and similarly if the fracture is close to the joint, it is necessary that the strap is on that location and on the joint itself.
Then align the fracture with a splint that is not too tight until the fracture is in its proper position, then it is refined by adjustments until it fits well. If there is no swelling or hotness, keep it as it is, but if there is swelling or heat, leave it for the seventh day and place on it a wet woolen cloth soaked with oil of almonds and rose water.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it, the treatment for tightening is to hold the splint on the fracture, then place a new piece of rope over the splint, then bind the arm to the body with the hand open on the bone.
The fracture and the blow affect the body and the limb, causing the limb to assume the position of a dislocated shoulder if it does not prevent you from doing so. If you fear that something may change for you due to the broken bone, use the splint.
Place the splint on the fracture, making it wider and stronger than other splints. Ensure that there is a space of one finger between each splint. Let the length of the splint be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from every side.
Then, you press on the splints with the pressure that was mentioned in the first chapter.
Make your pressure on the location of the fracture stronger.
Whenever after the fracture, you feel that the pressure is less, if you see that the splints and the pressure are as we said in the case of applying force to the limb within your time, then do it.
If you fear the hot pain, leave the pressure and the splint for a day as we said, then inspect the bandage every three days so that nothing happens in the place or swelling or blocking of food from reaching the limb due to the persistence of the pressure. It will heal everything as we mentioned.
If you are certain that nothing of this will occur, do not remove the sutures except after several days, and make it so that the cause of pain is on his back and his hand on the affected side, and place it under the body attached with a band filled with cotton made evenly.
Examine at all times, whether day or night, to ensure that the shape of the fractured bone does not heal improperly or the sutures break apart. Correct this thoroughly with your effort, and make the food for the patient on the level specified, which is that the food should be soft initially until the bones have solidified.
It is necessary that the patient's diet be softened, for it is common in bones and joints to heal in forty days. At that time, it is necessary to remove the sutures and use the warm bath to treat it with suitable ointments, for if the fracture is severe, it requires such treatment.
Do not remove the sutures from him for fifty days or two months.
The second chapter.
On the treatment of an arm fracture.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and the ulna, one smaller above and the other larger below it, so it is unlikely that only the larger bone will break or only the smaller bone will break, and even less likely that they will break together.
When the small upper bone (of the nose) is broken, its reduction becomes easier and quicker. When the lower bone is broken, breaking it is difficult and reducing it is laborious, and if both bones are broken together, then the bone that the small upper bone of the nose was attached to, is the one that was broken.
It is necessary for the physician, during reduction, to make the movement gentle until he sets it straight. If the large bone (of the nose) is broken, it is necessary to make the movement stronger. If both bones are broken, it is necessary to make the movement very strong.
The shape of the hand should be placed during reduction and the movement made slow on a sideways motion. The thumb should be above all fingers and the little finger below them, with the index finger raised slightly on top of it.
The position (of the fingers) should be such that it is not difficult for the index finger to reach upwards.
Then the servant holds it from below, either with his hand or with a rope, and another servant holds it from above in the same manner. The physician then sets the bone until it is set properly. If there are fragments of bone, they should be removed.
Apply every ulcer in its place with your effort, if you find an ulcer that is free and it penetrates the skin without being attached to it, then make a cut on it and remove it according to the method mentioned earlier; if the break occurs with a wound, you have amputated by mistake.
Take treatment from there, if it appears in the first layer of your skin and is a hot swelling; the leech should be applied with qirot al-maymooni (a kind of leech used for healing) made from the fat of a pig and white camphor and be between boiling and burning heat. Hold the leech gently until the swelling disappears.
Remove the leech and put the soft cloth soaked with ash from the oven mixed with white egg, then place the bladder and let it be the same size as the wound. Know that usually four bladders are placed on an arm.
If the break is in one tendon or in two together, then make your grip stronger and firmer on the site of the break. Every time you apply more pressure either upward or downward, make the pressure slightly longer as mentioned earlier.
To make the wound, which has been broken, soft and not too hard; the thread that sews it should be medium between coarse and fine as previously described, and the limb and suture should be inspected after several days.
"If something occurs that needs to be corrected, such as a foreign object in the limb, it is necessary to immerse the affected limb in clean warm water until the object settles and leave the limb undisturbed at night until it relaxes, then the wound should be sutured again.
"If the suturing has healed and the bone is broken or similar, correct it as much as possible, and also if food prevents the wound from healing, it is necessary to temporarily restrict food intake until the nutrients reach the limb, then resume suturing.
"If the limb is not affected by any of what we have mentioned, it should not be cut off except after forty days or a similar period, then tie the affected limb to its neck, keep the arm level and protect it from excessive movement, make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm usually heals in thirty or forty days, but sometimes it may take sixty-two days; all of this depends on the conditions of the patient and their strength.
The Third Chapter.
In treating the fracture of the hand and fingers.
The breaking of the wrist and sprain of the fingers is less common than actual fractures or dislocations; however, it is more common for the wrist to have a fracture or dislocation.
The patient should sit up with his back supported and in front of him a chair on a low stool.
Then he should put his hand in a cloth and the assistant holding the broken bones will adjust them as the physician sees fit until they fit together well; at that point, it is necessary to apply a splint and bandage unless there has been no swelling or excessive heat. A cushion made of fabric from above should then be placed on top according to the situation.
If the fracture is near the bottom of the wrist, make a small ball from cotton and tell the patient to hold it with his uninjured hand while a long splint is applied.
The cataplasma (jabirah) should be made from soft skin. Apply it to the affected area with all the fingers pressed together and press as necessary. If there is a tear toward the outside, make another cataplasma above and one below in the finger to keep the hand open.
Hold the finger as it rotates and interlace the fingers with a bandage. If the wound is between two fingertips, apply pressure as necessary then press with the hand. You may also wish to make a small cataplasma to keep the wound in place.
If the wound is on the side (wast) or the tip (sabbat), do not move it. Interlace with the proper finger or interlace all of them onto a splint. This is best, or place a small cataplasma on it.
As we said before, in the case of injury and abscess formation after cauterization, apply what is necessary to the inflammation whenever it occurs, as described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the Cauterization of Fractures.
Bone of the thigh frequently breaks and becomes palpable to touch as it twists toward front and back, its tightening being such that one bandage is applied above the fracture and another below, with the causative factor drawn out on its surface.
Then each bandage is directed to its side in accordance with this if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, and if it is near the origin of the thigh, it is necessary that a tight bandage be applied, either of thread or something similar.
At the origin of the thigh, towards the anus, the bandage should be directed above and the other bandage below the fracture, and similarly if the fracture is near the buttock, the bandage should be near the buttock for the downward direction, then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it matches the natural shape.
The bone is broken finely, so it is necessary to bear the burden and tightness unless a pain or fever occurs in the limb; if a fever occurs in it, leave it for some days until the sharp pain subsides, then return to treating it.
As for tightening, it is necessary to apply a strong and wide plaster to the fracture twice or thrice and leave some excess, then apply the arm until the bone reaches the origin of the joint and introduce a long string between the thigh and the leg close to the buttock from below; the ends of the string are left above on both sides.
Then turn to the vein and artery where there is an excess, then make a ligature at the site of the break, and from it make one ligature on the bone of the penis, then fill the gap that is between the artery and the penis with soft catgut so that it matches the tension.
Then begin with tightening the strength from the center at the site of the break, three or four times, and each time you have passed the ligature from the site of the break, your tension should be less, softer and shorter.
Then proceed to the ends of the ligature which you have inserted between the artery and the penis, tie them with more ligatures from above, then pass over the ends of the ligature downwards until it reaches the ankle, then tighten both sides of the ligatures from the other side as well.
In order for the ligature not to loosen from its place, then leave the tension on it as long as it does not cause the limb to eat, or be infected, or swell, or produce a smell and similar things, but if something of that happens, cure it and repair whatever has happened from it according to what we have previously mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a hard calcification in the bone that obstructs, it is necessary to remove it if possible, and otherwise break it and remove it, then treat the wound with what is similar to what has been previously mentioned until it heals. This break of the artery may be without adding the penis by ligatures.
According to what we mentioned in the limb and bone, except that its grip is thus, it does not expose itself to danger when pulling, even if it has been gripped, unless a finger is attached to it, in which case, its owner must always be careful.
The finger will thicken within fifty days, or it may increase slightly or decrease slightly, all of this is subject to the differences in conditions and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
On breaking a shinbone.
A shinbone fracture is relatively susceptible to breakage and is more susceptible to swelling. If it is prone to breaking, then it will either be split or fragmented in its parts, and this may occur with injury or without injury.
Place it thus (the broken shinbone) with care, grip it so that it reassembles the parts separated from its parts by the fingers until they join and become aligned, then apply a splint and put on a cast if necessary.
It will thicken from the place of the previous fracture, then all conditions that were described in other fractures such as inflammation and similar will meet and confront each other until every issue is resolved to the point of healing.
Sixteenth Chapter.
Breaking of the Bone: Treatise on Fractures.
The Bone of the Leg has Two Parts: One is Thick, called the Tibia, and the Other is Slender, known as the Fibula; both are Subject to Kinds of Fractures, except the Ulna [of the Arm]. And for this reason, the Bone-setting [technique] is the same for both [the Tibia and Fibula]; if both Bones are fractured, the Leg bends in all Directions; if the Slender Bone is fractured, the Leg bends towards the Front; if the Thick Bone, which happens from Below, it becomes evident to you.
The Rod, the Alignment and the Binding [of the bones] are to be applied according to that; except that it is necessary when the Fracture of the Leg is severe, with many Sharp Fragments, for the Rod to be Shorter and Lighter, and exert more Effort in the Setting.
In the Leg, there is something additional to the Arm; that when you have aligned and completed all your Work, take two [pieces of] Wool from a Broom Handle, which are used in Stuffing the Cushions between the Planks or are made of Reed Mats or similar.
Choose from it what has a slight toughness and not be brittle, and let its length be equal to that of the little finger from the knee down, then press on each one with two incisions along its length. Place one in the finger and the other on the opposite side.
From the knee down to the foot, then bind the splints in three places from both sides and the center. With this arrangement, the finger will not bend towards the right or north, but rather it will straighten out properly. A dowel of wood can be used of a length equal to that of the finger.
Place it [the dressing] in to preserve it from movement, and it is best to do so when there is a specific injury. Then examine the finger daily and pay close attention to it for prevention of infection or inflammation or anything like that. When something of this kind occurs, treat it with what is necessary to heal, and the bone of the finger may become stiff within thirty days or thereabouts.
The seventh chapter.
On the fracture of bones in the hand and fingers.
As for the carpus, it does not subject to fracture of the bone, and as for the bones of the leg, they may be subject to fracture, and the fingers also are less likely to be subjected to fracture but rather prone to rupture in most cases.
If the leg bone is exposed, it is broken and you see some of those bones have sharpened each other; place your foot on the ground flatly like a camel, then stand and place your foot on what has risen from those bones, then break and set it.
Until it returns to its position, then take the splints and bandages from above and place them under the sole of the foot on a small flat surface with two concave sides, then press firmly on it with force after breaking it with a lever and all that is needed.
If it lasts for three or four days, release the splint. You will find the bones have healed or are still broken; perform the work as you see fit. As for if any fingers are broken, force and set them according to what was described to you in the art of hand fracture.
Then make a splint for the broken fingers to their length, and let it project slightly from them. Then place this board that was described to you under the foot and press it firmly, for if any bones are broken in two or three or more.
Make on every nail a bezel of its size in a white socket and tighten the blade at the bottom of the foot, so that each end of the blade protrudes outside the leg to fix it properly.
It is important for you to remember everything we have mentioned regarding the fractures of all kinds and the various symptoms we have described, and to apply it accordingly for each symptom as required.
The Fourteenth Section.
Fracture of the woman's pelvis, temple bone, and man's bone (arm).
When a woman's pelvis is fractured, position her upright, then slightly tilt her to the side, and grab her from behind. Then, bind her hip with bandages until it fills and becomes like a ball in her pelvis.
Then, shake the woman and raise her slightly to make the bandages come out until it appears at the entrance of the pelvis like a ball and the fracture of the bone recovers. Then, make an opening on her abdomen whenever she desires to urinate, so that you can remove the bandages along with it until she urinates.
Put it back in the same way as you inserted it initially, then return to her original position. Do this for seven days or so, as it will heal, and if you wish to administer a suppository, insert a thin tube into her vagina and fill all of it with the suppository in her pelvis.
Then inflate the bladder with strength until it inflates in the abdomen, for when it breaks, it returns. Then sew it up with linen and let it heal for several days until it bursts open, and as for when the bone of the anus is broken in a man or woman, it is used in setting it.
And sew it as described in the bone setting, and it is not hidden from you that this strange fracture is rare and not easily found except for one who has some experience with my book, because the fracture itself indicates, in most cases.
On the path of correcting it (the fracture) and binding it, understand (it). As for a man when his anus is broken, take a hollow tube made of ox horn, enter it into him, then place a splint made of wood on it and let it remain for three or four days until it bursts open.
The Tenth Chapter.
On Bone Setting with Wounding.
It is necessary for one who has a fracture with wounding, and especially if the bone is large like the phalanx or shoulder blade or similar, to hasten its reduction as soon as possible when conditions for reduction are met as we have mentioned.
If the wound exudes blood, it is necessary to apply powdered cautery to it immediately, unless there is something else available. Then, take in your hand the fracture on that day with your eyes and do not delay it.
If no inflammation occurs, do nothing. But if inflammation occurs, leave the fracture for nine days until the hot inflammation subsides and do not approach it on the third or fourth day. For it is susceptible to harmful effects during this time.
If the fractured bone appears through the skin, it is necessary to bind and set it with your hand along with a soft splint. If you are unable to bind and set it with your hand, then use this instrument, which is made of iron, about the length of seven fingers or eight. Display it according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take from it three or four (pieces) in accordance with what is required for treatment in each type of fracture and make them rotatable so that there is a slight protrusion in it to prevent it from slipping during use at the time of treatment.
The edge should be sharp with a slight indentation at the end and it should be tapered towards the protrusion and from half of it to below, it should be very sharp. Here is its image:.
The surgery called Yunaniyyah Perm is intended to cause a small fracture, the edge of it should be sharp and meeting with the nearby bone, then push it back until the bone returns and levels some of the gaps, forming a setting for the fractured sides to align.
If the fractured side is sharp and not properly taken by the tool, it should be cut until the tool can grasp it. If it cannot be pushed back as described earlier, it should be cut at places that we have mentioned.
Share it with one of the saws as you can, then remove what remains in the bone of roughness and splinters. If the bone returns after being pushed back and the swelling appears after it and pain is severe, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to push it back to its natural position, you will be of great benefit to the patient. If you exhaust your strength with the bone, make a hole in it with a black potion specifically if you are in summer and do not put bandages or anything containing fat on the wound.
To prevent decay and corruption, use the forceps when you are free from setting the bone, leaving the wound open so it may heal by itself with pressure applied using the pledgets in the incisions according to the size of the wound.
Be very careful not to overstretch the wound during fracture, for often it is due to the ignorance of physicians that they cause either death or gangrene for their patients. Let your pressure be moderate and opposed to the pressure exerted by other fractures.
If the wound is shallow or large and you fear some minor adverse effects mentioned earlier, do not apply forceps and make incisions with a steel probe in the location of the forceps and bind it with them.
If after a day or two, you see that the wound is starting to produce pus, remove the probe placed in it with liquid, then use the scissors and probes which we commonly call surgical probes such as four-pronged probes and similar instruments, and you should ensure that the ligature is loosened.
You should inspect the wound every day at evening and morning until it closes, and you should ensure that the limb is set so that pus can easily drain from it. If the pus does not drain for several days and has not separated from the wound, know that there are small bone fragments present in it.
It is necessary to probe the wound with a probe, if there are any foreign bodies found, remove and extract them. If it is not free from such particles and it penetrates the tissue causing pain, form a swelling at the site of the injury, then you should remove it completely in all directions.
If there is any wound, ulcer or other kind of damage and defect in the tissue, it is necessary to address each type with the treatment prescribed for it, describing it in its respective chapter.
It is important to remain with the patient and prepare your mind if a major bone is broken, such as the femur or tibia and fibula from the large bones, for you should not attempt to pull it together nor extract it. Often, death occurs in such cases, but you should leave it to heal naturally, for perhaps after twenty or thirty days, it will reattach itself by itself.
If you see a place in the wound suitable for treatment, then treat it, otherwise leave it alone.
The Tenth Chapter.
Treatment of Contracture that Occurs due to Some Fractures.
This contracture often occurs due to immobility following a fracture, and it is seldom far from the joint, causing the shape of the limb to be distorted and perhaps preventing natural function.
If the swelling is linear, use medications that have a constricting effect such as scammony, lupine, madder, opium poppy, almonds and similar substances. Take one or all of them, knead with a binding liquid such as vinegar, white of egg or gum.
Apply it on the swelling with strong pressure and leave the pressure for several days until it dissolves, then remove it and repeat if necessary until the swelling disappears, God willing, or apply a hard piece of lead to it.
If the lead has a special property that dissolves in all tissues, if the swelling becomes hardened and tightens the need to remove it, break it from above and cut off the surrounding tissue or soften it with some tools until it dissipates and heals the wound until it is cured.
The 21st Chapter.
In the treatment of fractures, if it becomes calloused and remains rough after that.
If a bone fracture heals and the limb remains rough and weak after that, it is due to many causes. One of them may be due to excessive healing of ligaments and tying them on something inappropriate, or due to overtightening of ligaments to prevent food from reaching the limb, or due to excessive and irregular swelling, or due to irregular movements at the wrong time, or due to little blood in the patient's body and weakness.
The treatment for this is to feed the patient and make him exercise until blood increases in his body, then use the cupping glass and insert the suction tube into it along with leeches and similar things, then apply leeches on the limb to draw nourishment towards it or keep the swelling by water. until food flows through it and returns to its natural shape.
The second and fortieth chapter.
In the treatment of broken bones, if they become swollen and are immobilized improperly.
When a limb has been fractured and then healed with swelling or the broken bone becomes twisted or distorted in that manner. Unless the limb does not prevent its natural movement, it is not permissible for someone to claim that he can break a bone from the head, as many ignorant physicians and enforcers do in our country.
This action is highly dangerous, leading to a great risk of infection, but if the wound and swelling are straight, it is necessary to clean it with boiling water in which aromatic herbs such as papyrus leaves, rose oil, ambergris, and similar have been boiled.
It is necessary to use the sharp, well-made diachylon or take the aromatic substance of papyrus leaves, beat it with chicken fat and sheep oil, and apply it, or take the tyn dusm and crush it with soap powder, among other medicines known as "napqadh al-andimal.
When swelling occurs, it is necessary to massage the area continuously and gently with a smooth, thin needle that should always be in hand, moving the limb in all directions during each occasion. If the swellings have settled and worsened and hardened and require treatment with iron.
It is necessary to cut off the swelling and separate the bone and remove any excess swelling or bone with incisions of moderate width, using forceps in the process with care and caution, then treat the wound as described until it heals.
Chapter Three and Forty.
The jaw is a joint that leaves its place and becomes fixed, preventing movement, causing the shape of the bone to change, and resulting in injuries or severe pain. Therefore, when anyone has a jaw problem, it is necessary for him to go immediately to its reduction.
The patient should not wait because if he delays the pain and swelling of the place will make it harder to reduce the jaw, so he should not delay, move or touch in case of pain as it often leads to numbness and harmful injuries.
If such a case arises, it is necessary to go to the cause of the problem and then wait until the swelling subsides slightly, then immerse the bone in hot water and oil, then reduce it with help and treat each bone as indicated in its place.
The bones of the jaw are also arranged from top to bottom of the body.
The Fourth and Fifth Jaw Bone.
Treatment of a broken lower jaw.
If the jaws are dislocated, they will only be able to be put back in place in a state of anesthesia, and their removal will be on one or two sides, either by sliding them gently back into place or completely removing them until they return. The excerpt translates to: "Until reaching the chest, so as not to inflict pain on the patient and he is unable to grasp it or can't endure pressure, thus he will speak with his tongue, if it comes out easily, then it usually returns more smoothly.
"If the fracture is complete, it should be returned promptly and not delayed, and the servant should hold the head of the patient while the doctor inserts his one hand into the mouth of the fractured jaw from one side.
"Or both his hands are inserted if the jaw is from both sides, and all his fingers from outside straighten it, and he (the patient) is ordered to open his mouth wide and let go of the jaw, and the doctor straightens the jaw and pushes it back to its original position.
"If the return is difficult and if both jaws are fractured, then hot oil or fat is used to facilitate their return, as we have mentioned, and they should not be delayed in returning them. Once they return and align, and if the jaw does not lock or dislocate again, then a plaster made of clay, lard and wool is applied.
Then bind it with a ligature of silk, and let its head and neck be tucked between two linen folds so that it does not move to the right, left, or north. It should not exert pressure on any organ but make its food liquid.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is fixed, let him eat whatever he desires without using a probe or holding his mouth open during eating and drinking and speaking until the jaw is fixed and the swelling subsides.
If the jaws are locked and they do not move to their respective places, it often happens that pain, permanent swelling, or a bulging belly occurs, and sometimes the abdomen swells and tightens in several places. If you see this, know that it is damaged.
And often a person dies from that within ten days.
The fifth and fortieth section.
In the treatment of jaw dislocation and fracture of the mandible.
As for the dislocation, it does not move from the internal side due to its connection with the chest and it may dislocate outward, which can be felt by touch, and it must be immobilized by the patient lying on his back and extending his arms.
Then press the site with your fist with force, it will return. Then place a ligature and suture on it and tighten it. As for its side that faces the incision and connects to it, it should not be cut except in the vagina. If it is cut, it should be stitched back and prepared as we have mentioned before and what suits you.
Then place a ligature and suture on it and tighten it. The patient should be instructed to press the wound and keep it pressed until it stops bleeding. With this treatment, if the side of the incision separates from its place, it will return to its position.
Chapter 6 and 20.
On the treatment of wound separation.
Know that a wound can separate in three ways: first, it can separate towards the belly button downwards; second, it can separate towards the chest; and third, it can separate above the wound, but this occurs only in the vagina.
And a wound does not separate towards the back near the armpit or towards the front near the shoulder, and it usually separates and comes out downwards towards the belly button, especially in those with less fat as it comes out quickly from them and enters quickly.
As for those with numerous veins, it signifies that he will exit through an incision and enter through an incision. It may be misleading to some people due to a blow or fall causing a twitch or a warm swelling, which might lead them to believe that he has been injured. However, it is necessary to verify this until the truth is established.
When he is to be treated, the injury will become evident if it is below the navel and it touches the inflamed vein and the healthy one. You will find a difference between them externally, and you will find the head of the injured vein with a hole and under the navel when touched, the head of the injured vein feels like a hard ball.
The paralysis cannot make him raise his hand to the ear nor move it in all movements, and likewise if the injury is around the chest or above, you will find that clearly by touch which cannot be hidden.
This paralysis can be cured if it is dry or if the patient is a child. To cure it, you should place your hand under his navel and raise the affected part forcefully to its position above with the servant raising his hand and stretching it above.
Then he puts it below and it returns quickly; if it does not return as mentioned, and it has been a long time since the bite happened, it is necessary to investigate the cause in hot water and use a leech that draws and lets blood like boiling onions, garlic, and licorice in hot water and use it.
Then the cause is placed on its surface and a ball made of moderately strong cotton is put under it, then the physician places his finger in the hole of the ball and holds the cause with his hand and lets it fall down.
The ball is pushed with the foot; it returns to its place, and if you wish to return it to this side and it is necessary that a man taller than the cause stands by his side and stops him from one side and enters from below the cotton of the cause and lifts the cotton above until the cause is hanging in the air. The last attendant attracts the cause's hand to below with his stomach, if the cause is light, it is necessary to attach something else to it to make it heavier because the bite returns in its time; it may also return on another side.
The translation is: "Place a long stick with its head shaped like the end of a hawk's beak, neither too heavy nor too light, under the patient after placing an incision on the stick, then the patient and the stick remain in position for the length of the stick.
"Then move your hand to the opposite side and push the body from the other side with force, so that the dislocation returns quickly to its place. If it does not return, use this treatment which is to take a stick of similar length as the arm. "Present it (the arm) with a length of four fingers and thicken it to two fingers, with a head shaped for easy insertion into the incision in the thigh on this figure:
"Then tie a smooth incision on the pointed end to prevent the stick from hurting the patient, then he should press down under the thigh and pull both the hand and arm towards the stick, tying the stick to the limb and using assistance and pressure on the side of the hand.
"Then place the arm on a flat surface. Align it and move the hand downwards, leaving the rest of the body suspended from the other side so that the dislocation enters from any direction. If it enters on one side, a ball made of yarn should be placed under the thigh to balance the pressure.
Then, he should load the plaster made from the embers of the mill with lime and white of egg on all sides while it is being turned from above. Then, he should press the ball from below the sheep's bladder firmly and smoothly, and rotate the bandage over it from above and attach his hand to its neck.
He should leave his hand immobile for seven days, and it is preferable to reduce the patient's food intake until the limb strengthens, as it heals faster. Then, after seven or five days, he should start moving it. If it remains fixed without any pain, then it has been cured.
If the severed part continues to ooze frequently due to moisture or another reason, it is necessary to use three astringents in each opening of the wound, as this prevents infection. After seven days have passed and the bandage has been removed, if the severed part does not heal.
If the bandage and pressure are applied to it multiple times but do not heal, if it falls off and causes pain and is unable to be raised above, know that the severed part in the end of the limb has been cut or stretched or injured. Know this when the severed part does not heal in its place forever.
If a fracture occurs near the jaw, chest, and back, it should be reduced by using pressure and pulling with hands until it returns to its original position. All kinds of force and treatment should be applied until it heals. If after reduction, the limb appears deformed and stiff in movement.
The cauterizing iron should not be applied repeatedly until the deformity disappears and it returns to its original state.
Chapter Seven and Forty.
Treatment of Fracture Dislocation.
When dislocating a fracture, it should be dislocated forcefully and likewise returned forcefully. It dislocates in all directions, and it is not hidden from you that this occurs beneath the sight and touch in any form.
If a fracture dislocation is correctly set, it will be clearly indicated by the swelling at the site of the fracture and it will not be able to bend or touch the affected limb. It is important that you should try to move the fracture from your side before exposing it to heat and cold.
If exposed to heat and cold, it will be difficult to move the fracture and most likely it will not heal. If the fracture is at the back, it will cause more pain and frequently accompanied by death.
If a finger can be manipulated to return to its place with the hand and arm relaxed, and the physician applies pressure from above and below, he should push the separated part either with all his fingers or with the tip of his thumb until it returns to its original position.
If a tooth is loose, the hand should be squeezed until the tip of the thumb touches it, and if it does not return to its place, exert strong pressure with both hands, making the arms act like a pliers.
The assistant should hold both hands to prevent it from slipping during manipulation, then the arm is rotated in all directions after striking its palm with a wad of cotton or a thin cloth. If the physician pushes the separated part with his hand, he should anoint it with oil so that it can be easily retracted.
The separated part should be pushed back forcefully until it returns, and after its return, a bandage should be applied to it with a plaster that has been dried and whitened with egg white, and the arm is tightly bound from the neck of the assistant and left for some days.
Then, if it (the separation) is firm in its place, unloosen the sutures from it and leave it, but if you see that the separation has not worsened, prepare the suture and the sutures again and leave it for days until it does worsen, then unloosen it. If something similar happens to him after the separation returns and it is unloosened.
If it (the wound) becomes tight in movement, do not manipulate the wound in the bath and use gentle massage and tapping until it softens or apply warm oil on the separation then suture it and leave it for a day and night then remove it and put him in the bath, if it swells, make an incision on the separation with a balanced force.
Then, make an incision on him multiple times and the second and third time with entry to the bath until it softens, if you wish, put wet wool soaked in oil on him and apply pressure on it several times, for it will soften and return to its original state.
The eighth and tenth (sections).
On the treatment of fractures.
A fracture of the hand is often difficult to reduce and set, but it is necessary to hasten the reduction of the fracture in the hour when it occurs before the place is exposed or heated.
The servant should apply pressure on the wound with a flat piece of bone, and the physician shall place his hand on the separated part and push it back, unless it is necessary to ensure that the fracture has returned to the inside of the hand.
The wound should be covered with a flat piece of bone, and the fracture shall protrude outward if it is on the surface of the hand. In such a case, the physician's palm shall be placed on the same side as the fracture, and if it returns to its place, no additional pressure is needed; otherwise, it should be compressed with a splint for pain relief.
Leave it alone and do not try to reset it as it is unlikely to be reset after several days, unless the fracture remains on a curve and causes no harm to the hand except for swelling, in which case it would not be possible to grasp anything.
If the bone is broken or dislocated, there is no solution except to compress it firmly, although this may or may not be helpful; if the fracture has been reset, apply a splint as described earlier, then compress and wait for five days.
Then soften and exercise the hand, if it seizes up or shows something abnormal. Wash it with hot water and massage repeatedly until it becomes soft.
The ninth and tenth sections.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, and if it gets dislocated towards the back of the hand or inside, extend the finger and apply pressure on the joint until it returns to its place, then tie the end of the finger and bind it towards the side it was dislocated to.
Leave it for a couple of days, then release and stretch it until it adjusts to its normal state during the day. If it is night-time, bind it according to the same method without ceasing to soften and exercise it by movement, and bind it at night until it improves. Do this for as many days as necessary until it strengthens.
And likewise, treat it if it gets dislocated towards the inside of the hand by binding it towards the same side and perform the same actions until it escapes, and likewise do the same if it gets dislocated in any other direction.
The thirty-third section.
On the treatment of cervical dislocation.
When the fissure occurs in the back or neck vertebrae, or when many hernias leave their places, there is no cure because death hastens towards the ill one.
A sign of this is when the pus (brz) comes out without intention and one cannot stop it, frequently spurting from some of his limbs either arms or legs or one of them, and if a hernia (khirzah) leaves its place, then it often becomes incurable.
The hernia (khirzah) can become incurable in four directions; if it occurs at the back, it is called a "herniated disc" (hadbah). Its treatment is that one should observe if the herniated disc has occurred from an injury or not. If it has occurred from an injury, then there is no cure for it nor any relief of pain (br'at al-batthah), but if it occurs due to a fall or similar circumstances, then...
The initial descriptions of the hernia (khirzah) in long speech have no more benefit than what I have conveyed briefly from it, explaining and clarifying its meaning and depicting the instrument contrary to what has been clarified and explained.
I say that the abscess, which occurs in front of the chest, has no remedy or relief from it, and similarly, the one on both sides (flanks). However, treatment is only applicable for the abscess occurring in the back, as I have described it, and this is to press on its surface.
On a level surface close to the wall, spread some moist material so as not to harm its chest then place an upright wooden piece in a hole on one side of the surface near its head and another wooden piece on the other side of the surface near its feet.
A servant holds the wooden piece and keeps it firm in the hole, while another servant holds the other one in place in this position. Then, they strike the patient's chest and cover him with a soft, durable cloth and tie its edge to the wooden piece that is near his head.
Then, the other cloth is shaken over and across his back and over his buttocks and neck, then all the ropes are gathered and tied together at the second wooden piece that is near his feet. Each servant pulls the rope towards their respective wooden piece without letting the wooden pieces move from their fixed positions.
Except that he does not press his hand firmly on the bladder until it returns, or he places a flat stone on it then remains on the stone with his feet until it returns, for if it does not return with this treatment, he takes a flat stone whose length is about three spans.
He carves a hole in the wall that is near the patient, and places one edge of the flat stone into it, then he positions the center of the stone on the bladder and the physician places his feet on the other edge, and he presses hard until he feels the bladder being pressed.
It returns to its place, and if you wish to make it with the hands, you can hang two wooden poles of a span each near the patient at the end of the room, and also place two poles at his feet in the same way.
It should be between each wooden pole a distance of two fingers, and he makes a hole in each pole where the catheter will pass, and he embeds all the poles into the ground so that the bladder does not move and a rotating catheter is inserted into the holes in the wooden poles.
On one side of the wound, a splint has been inserted that measures about one cubit, and in the other two wooden pieces similar to it, then tighten the bandage that you have tied around the handle at the top of the syringe and the bands that you have tied around its neck.
Then, stand beside each syringe with an assistant who shakes his hand with the syringe filled with the contents. The physician adjusts the plunger to the required amount and this is a picture of the syringe, needle, and handle.
Then, after the incision is made and the position has returned to normal, it is necessary to place the dry cotton soaked in egg white, followed by the suturing. From above the cotton, a strip of wood must be placed that is about three fingers wide or similar and long enough to contain the wound.
On some of the correct sutures, then tie the bandage as desired and use the mild food until it softens, if any pus remains in the wound at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to use the medicines that heal and soothe with the use of the wood strip described earlier for a long time.
In that, a lead sheet is used, and it may expose an ulcer at the end of the backbone, thinking it is a protruding bone; however, do not expose yourself to this treatment, for it might possibly cause death.
Chapter One, Thirty.
Treatment of the Herniated Vertebra.
Know that the intervertebral disc and interarticular disc only expose to a fracture, but they do not expose to any of the other types of soft injuries such as ruptures or compressions. The intervertebral disc protrudes on four sides.
